Document 61 of 108

How school name debate became so contentious; Explainer

Author: Macleod, Bri an

Publication info: Macleod, Brian. 

https://libproxy.wlu.ca/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/how-school-name-debate-became-so-contentious/docview/2933686512/se-2?accountid=15090

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://ocul-wlu.primo.exlibrisgroup.com/discovery/openurl?institution=01OCUL_WLU&amp;vid=01OCUL_WLU:WLU_DEF&amp;?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Acanadiannews&amp;atitle=How+school+name+debate+became+so+contentious%3B+Explainer&amp;title=The+Windsor+Star&amp;issn=&amp;date=2024-03-01&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.1&amp;au=Macleod%2C+Brian&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=The+Windsor+Star&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: The uproar over choosing the name for a new Kingsville school may seem like it came out of nowhere, but the table was set for this kind of reaction - all someone had to do was spill the milk.
Kingsville trustee Julia Burgess likely knew she was tipping over the glass when she introduced the name Erie Migration Academy at the Feb. 20 board meeting of the Greater Essex County District School Board.
Trustees had done this before, in Amherstburg, in 2021. The reasons were different but the process, and community response, was similar.
"Amherstburg School Naming Committee in Turmoil," read the Sept. 21, 2021, headline in The Windsor Star.
As construction of a new school to replace General Amherst High School was underway, a 16-member committee recommended the name Amherst District, ironically citing Kingsville District as its influence. But the board's superintendent refused to forward that name to trustees.
Lord Jeffrey Amherst was a bad man. A British army commander in the 18th century, he liked the idea of distributing blankets laced with smallpox to "inoculate the Indians" in order to "extirpate this execrable race."
The naming committee, however, was thinking about community identity. Amid the ensuing outrage, trustee Ron LeClair stepped down as head of the naming committee.
The name eventually chosen: North Star High School.
So trustees likely knew at their Feb. 20 board meeting what the reaction might be when they cast aside the two names recommended by the Kingsville school naming committee - Kingsville District Academy and Greater Kingsville Academy - in favour of Erie Migration Academy.
In introducing the name at the board meeting, Burgess said the committee work had been "controversial" because it concerned the replacement of six schools. She said it was a "hot topic" in town and in the municipalities of affected feeder schools.
Trustee Sarah Cipkar said before the vote that it was a "bold but fitting choice for the area," and she supported Burgess's proposed name. But she also offered that "I would hate for trustee Burgess to come away from this in a negative way."
Trustee Connie Buckler, who also liked the name, asked Burgess: "Is there going to be a great deal of backlash over this new name?" Burgess replied: "I'll be truthful.
I think there are effigies being made and burning by tomorrow. I think it's going to be highly contentious, and I think that people are going to say things.
"They will say that this was cooked, that this was made up ahead of time, that we were going to do something different all the way along. You wasted my time and why did I go to meetings and come up with these suggestions only for you to ignore them and throw them out? "And it will feel like the committee is disrespected. That's not my intention."
Burgess is an enthusiastic participant and an engaging conversationalist who chaired the Kingsville naming committee, but by her own admission she is not good at "the elevator pitch."
She says her thoughts tend to come out in a stream-of-consciousness manner.
That comes across in watching the board's debate and reading her letter in response to MPP Anthony Leardi (PC - Essex) calling for trustees to reconsider their decision on the new name.
Burgess's garrulous style was apparent in her presentation to the board. There was mention of migrations involving birds, butterflies, dragonflies, students, migrant workers and Indigenous people.
We heard that the word "academy" is Greek, referring to an institution of higher learning. We heard that Erie is a short form of an Indigenous word from the Haudenosaunee language meaning "long tail," which could be Long Point in Lake Erie or a long lake.
Burgess's presentation convinced her colleagues, but it's likely that most people in Kingsville didn't hear her impassioned, though somewhat labyrinthine, pitch.
What the naming committee heard was that their work and recommendations were ignored, and done quickly, and easily dismissed.
The Kingsville high school naming committee consisted of 24 people, including students from four schools, principals, board staff and community members. Positions were advertised, suggestions were sought, all in a public manner, with stories in the media.
The committee met twice. Student focus groups were formed.
The process and the composition of the committee naturally left the impression the search for a school name was a comprehensive and credible process.
Expectations were high. There is a photo circulating on social media of a white board with 18 suggested names, nine of them crossed out.
There is another photo of a laminated poster with eight suggested names, whittled down from the original 600. Lake Erie Migration Academy and Kingsville Migration Academy are both still alive on both those visuals.
So, when Burgess introduced the name Erie Migration Academy to trustees, she felt she had her foot firmly in the debate at the naming committee.
But remember the Amherstburg debacle. The committee's suggested name represented the school's history and community identity.
And so it was in Kingsville. The town is not named as a form of royal or empire adulatory. It's named after one of the first settlers, James King, who built the first house in the area and, according to the online Canadian Encyclopedia, was instrumental in building the first school in 1843.
You can draw a direct line between the Town of Kingsville, the name of Kingsville District High School, and more than 180 years of local history.
The name Erie Migration has history, yes, but it's more philosophical, as Burgess said.
Residents took to social media to vent.
They did so with great zeal.
Leardi, the area's representative at Queen's Park, entered the debate, saying in his letter to the board the name "means nothing to no one," and Kingsville Mayor Dennis Rogers spoke on the issue at a recent council meeting.
"We've seen the social media comments, we've seen emails and messages from people in the community who are very unhappy with the name chosen for the new Kingsville school," Rogers said.
Kingsville residents wanted Kingsville in the name and no explanation of how the new school is really a catchment facility for Harrow, Colchester and Pelee Island was going to assuage them.
Their protest Feb. 23 in front of Kingsville District High School brought a noisy deluge of honking horns from drivers passing by.
And then there are the discussions on social media about the acronym EMA - likely to be worn on school team uniforms - and its unfortunate urban slang meaning.
Dismissing the name Kingsville and the efforts of the committee was a one-two punch Kingsville refuses to accept.
Another protest was announced for March 1. And there is now discussion of a name change consideration at the board's next meeting on March 19.
Unlike Amherstburg, there is no ugly history associated with the name Kingsville. There is only the history first started by James King that still resonates around the community, and there are the efforts of the 24 people and the time invested by all those who suggested 600 names.
Trustees may be fine with Erie Migration Academy and stand their ground.
But the community might continue to feel differently. bamacleod@postmedia.com

Subject: Names; Meetings; Committees; Community; Migration; Secondary schools; Trustees; Elevator pitch; Social networks; School boards

Document 62 of 108

Will pharmacare actually benefit Canadians?

Author: Kheiriddin, Tasha

Publication info: Kheiriddin, Tasha. 

https://libproxy.wlu.ca/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/will-pharmacare-actually-benefit-canadians/docview/2933686500/se-2?accountid=15090

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://ocul-wlu.primo.exlibrisgroup.com/discovery/openurl?institution=01OCUL_WLU&amp;vid=01OCUL_WLU:WLU_DEF&amp;?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=unknown&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Acanadiannews&amp;atitle=Will+pharmacare+actually+benefit+Canadians%3F&amp;title=The+Windsor+Star&amp;issn=&amp;date=2024-03-01&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.7&amp;au=Kheiriddin%2C+Tasha&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=The+Windsor+Star&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: Is pharmacare doomed to fail? Last week, Quebec announced that it would opt out of the Liberal-NDP plan for a national pharmacare program. This week, it was Alberta's turn. Health Minister Adriana LaGrange blasted the lack of provincial consultation and accused the federal government of overstepping its jurisdiction.
"We're not going to allow Ottawa to pick and choose what coverage should be available to Albertans based on issues they find politically palatable."
Instead of taking orders from Ottawa, Alberta will take the cash.
"Give us the dollars," LaGrange said. "Allow us to enhance the programs we actually have now, rather than create more bureaucracy."
In response, NDP Leader Jagmeet Singh attacked Quebec Premier François Legault and Alberta Premier Danielle Smith.
"I'm not surprised at the conservative governments that have cut investments in health care. These are the same premiers that, when you look at their province, they made massive cuts that hurt workers and patients."
Ah, the C-word. Never mind that Legault's government doesn't go by that name: it's a convenient excuse to attack the ideology, and by extension the federal Conservatives as well. Singh is eager to savour his pharmacare "victory" and position the NDP as the true alternative to the Tories. And since he has as much a chance of gaining seats in Quebec as he does in Alberta, bashing those two governments is a low-risk strategy.
But the NDP aren't the only ones playing politics with this deal. The Liberals are as well, starting with the choice of contraceptive and diabetes medication as the first drugs to be covered.
Contraception is a relatively low-cost item for the feds: it's already fully covered in B.C. and Manitoba, and partly covered in Ontario. Most private health plans - which four in five Canadians have - pay for it as well. More importantly, the coverage appeals to a key voter demographic - women - that the Liberals need to get back. The latest polls show that 36 per cent would vote Conservative, compared to 26 per cent who would vote Liberal.
As for diabetes, that is also a numbers game.
Nearly 10 per cent of Canadians - six million people - suffer from the disease, with an additional six million classified as "pre-diabetic." Rates are even higher among seniors (20 per cent) and First Nations populations on reserve (17 per cent). Almost everyone knows someone with diabetes and knows that insulin is a lifesaver.
But that doesn't mean you need universal pharmacare to cover people who can't afford it. It's like bringing in a $27-billion bazooka to kill a fly. A study released last week by the Montreal Economic Institute found that only 2.8 per cent of Canadians currently have no drug coverage whatsoever. Twenty-five million people have private insurance, and existing provincial plans subsidize or cover a host of medications for persons who do not, including low-income patients and seniors.
The study also confirmed that imposing a single-payer universal drug insurance plan would jeopardize the quality of coverage for at least 21.5 million people. That's because a single-payer model would not fund many medications currently covered by private plans, including innovative medicines.
Based on the experiences of New Zealand, cutting edge drugs won't be introduced into the Canadian market at all, because a universal plan would deem them too costly. A 2019 report commissioned by Medicines New Zealand ranked the country last for access to medicines and pharmaceutical investment among 20 OECD nations. Of 304 medicines introduced from 2011 to 2017, New Zealand funded only 17. Life-saving cancer therapies and other innovative medicines were rendered unavailable.
In other words, national single-payer pharmacare will consign Canadians to the same problems as national single-payer medicare: lack of choice, rationing of care, and worse outcomes. Instead of keeping what works and fixing what's broken, the NDP chose to score political points, and the Liberals bought themselves another year in power.
So kudos to Alberta and Quebec for standing up and opting out. Let's hope other provinces join them and expose this deal for what it is: a political game that leaves patients in the cold. Tasha Kheiriddin is Postmedia's national politics columnist.

Subject: Diabetes; Canadians; Indigent care

Location: New Zealand; Canada; Quebec Canada

Identifier / keyword: pharmacare; doomed; quebec; announced; national; program

Document 63 of 108

Explainer: How Kingsville school name debate became so contentious

Author: MacLeod, Brian

Publication info: MacLeod, Brian. 

https://libproxy.wlu.ca/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/blogs-podcasts-websites/explainer-how-kingsville-school-name-debate/docview/2933491270/se-2?accountid=15090

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://ocul-wlu.primo.exlibrisgroup.com/discovery/openurl?institution=01OCUL_WLU&amp;vid=01OCUL_WLU:WLU_DEF&amp;?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Acanadiannews&amp;atitle=Explainer%3A+How+Kingsville+school+name+debate+became+so+contentious&amp;title=The+Windsor+Star+%28Online%29&amp;issn=&amp;date=2024-02-29&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=&amp;au=MacLeod%2C+Brian&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=The+Windsor+Star+%28Online%29&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: The uproar over choosing the name for a new Kingsville school may seem like it came out of nowhere, but the table was set for this kind of reaction - all someone had to do was spill the milk. Kingsville Trustee Julia Burgess likely knew she was tipping over the glass when she introduced the name 'Erie Migration Academy' at the Feb. 20 board meeting of the Greater Essex County District School Board. Trustees had done this before, in Amherstburg, in 2021. The reasons were different but the process, and community response, was similar. 'Amherstburg school naming committee in turmoil,' read the Sept. 21, 2021, headline in The Windsor Star. As construction of a new school to replace General Amherst High School was underway, a 16-member committee recommended the name Amherst District, ironically citing Kingsville District as its influence. But the board's superintendent refused to forward that name to trustees. Lord Jeffrey Amherst was a bad man. A British army commander in the 18th century, he liked the idea of distributing blankets laced with smallpox to "inoculate the Indians" in order to "extirpate this execrable race." The naming committee, however, was thinking about community identity. Amidst the ensuing outrage, trustee Ron LeClair stepped down as head of the naming committee. The name eventually chosen: North Star High School. So trustees likely knew at their Feb. 20 board meeting what the reaction might be when they cast aside the two names recommended by the Kingsville school naming committee - Kingsville District Academy and Greater Kingsville Academy - in favour of Erie Migration Academy. In introducing the name at the board meeting, Burgess said the committee work had been "controversial" because it concerned the replacement of six schools. She said it was a "hot topic" in town and in the municipalities of affected feeder schools. Trustee Sarah Cipkar said before the vote that it was a "bold but fitting choice for the area," and she supported Burgess's proposed name. But she also offered that "I would hate for trustee Burgess to come away from this in a negative way." Trustee Connie Buckler, who also liked the name, asked Burgess: "Is there going to be a great deal of backlash over this new name?" Burgess replied: "I'll be truthful. I think there are effigies being made and burning by tomorrow. I think it's going to be highly contentious, and I think that people are going to say things. "They will say that this was cooked, that this was made up ahead of time, that we were going to do something different all the way along. You wasted my time and why did I go to meetings and come up with these suggestions only for you to ignore them and throw them out? "And it will feel like the committee is disrespected. That's not my intention." Burgess is an enthusiastic participant and an engaging conversationalist who chaired the Kingsville naming committee, but by her own admission she is not good at "the elevator pitch." She says her thoughts tend to come out in a stream-of-consciousness manner. That comes across in watching the board's debate and reading her letter in response to MPP Anthony Leardi (PC - Essex) calling for trustees to reconsider their decision on the new name. Burgess's garrulous style was apparent in her presentation to the board. There was mention of migrations involving birds, butterflies, dragonflies, students, migrant workers and Indigenous people. We heard that the word 'academy' is Greek, referring to an institution of higher learning. We heard that Erie is a short form of an Indigenous word from the Haudenosaunee language meaning 'long tail,' which could be Long Point in Lake Erie or a long lake. Burgess's presentation convinced her colleagues, but it's likely that most people in Kingsville didn't hear her impassioned, though somewhat labyrinthine, pitch. What the naming committee heard was that their work and recommendations were ignored, and done quickly, and easily dismissed. The Kingsville high school naming committee consisted of 24 people, including students from four schools, principals, board staff and community members. Positions were advertised, suggestions were sought, all in a public manner, with stories in the media. The committee met twice. Student focus groups were formed. The process and the composition of the committee naturally left the impression the search for a school name was a comprehensive and credible process. Expectations were high. There is a photo circulating on social media of a white board with 18 suggested names, nine of them crossed out. There is another photo of a laminated poster with eight suggested names, whittled down from the original 600. Lake Erie Migration Academy and Kingsville Migration Academy are both still alive on both those visuals. So, when Burgess introduced the name Erie Migration Academy to trustees, she felt she had her foot firmly in the debate at the naming committee. But remember the Amherstburg debacle. The committee's suggested name represented the school's history and community identity. And so it was in Kingsville. The town is not named as a form of royal or empire adulatory. It's named after one of the first settlers, James King, who built the first house in the area and, according to the online Canadian Encyclopedia, was instrumental in building the first school in 1843. You can draw a direct line between the Town of Kingsville, the name of Kingsville District High School, and more than 180 years of local history. The name Erie Migration has history, yes, but it's more philosophical, as Burgess said. Residents took to social media to vent. They did so with great zeal. Leardi, the area's representative at Queen's Park, entered the debate, saying in his letter to the board the name "means nothing to no one," and Kingsville Mayor Dennis Rogers spoke on the issue at a recent council meeting.
"We've seen the social media comments, we've seen emails and messages from people in the community who are very unhappy with the name chosen for the new Kingsville school," Rogers said.
Kingsville residents wanted Kingsville in the name and no explanation of how the new school is really a catchment facility for Harrow, Colchester and Pelee Island was going to assuage them. Their protest Feb. 23 in front of Kingsville District High School brought a noisy deluge of honking horns from drivers passing by. And then there are the discussions on social media about the acronym EMA - likely to be worn on school team uniforms - and its unfortunate urban slang meaning. Related
Trustee seeks police advice after threats over Kingsville school name
Kingsville District High School students, parents protest new name
Public board names new Kingsville, Tecumseh schools
Jarvis: The public school board just nailed a teachable moment
Dismissing the name Kingsville and the efforts of the committee was a one-two punch Kingsville refuses to accept. Another protest was announced for March 1. And there is now discussion of a name change consideration at the board's next meeting on March 19. Unlike Amherstburg, there is no ugly history associated with the name Kingsville. There is only the history first started by James King that still resonates around the community, and there are the efforts of the 24 people and the time invested by all those who suggested 600 names. Trustees may be fine with Erie Migration Academy and stand their ground. But the community might continue to feel differently. bamacleod@postmedia.com

Subject: Names; Meetings; Committees; Students; Community; Migration; Secondary schools; Trustees; Elevator pitch; Social networks; School boards

Business indexing term: Subject: Trustees Elevator pitch Social networks

Document 64 of 108

Want to race in the mille?; Bring money & patience and you could enter one of the world's premier vintage events

Author: Harper, Brian

Publication info: Harper, Brian. 

https://libproxy.wlu.ca/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/want-race-mille-bring-money-amp-patience-you/docview/2933288323/se-2?accountid=15090

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://ocul-wlu.primo.exlibrisgroup.com/discovery/openurl?institution=01OCUL_WLU&amp;vid=01OCUL_WLU:WLU_DEF&amp;?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Acanadiannews&amp;atitle=Want+to+race+in+the+mille%3F%3B+Bring+money+%26amp%3B+patience+and+you+could+enter+one+of+the+world%27s+premier+vintage+events&amp;title=The+Windsor+Star&amp;issn=&amp;date=2024-02-29&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=SR.6&amp;au=Harper%2C+Brian&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=The+Windsor+Star&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: The recent release of the movie Ferrari, based on the unflinching 1991 biography Enzo Ferrari: The Man, the Cars, the Races, the Machine, by motorsport journalist Brock Yates, has again shed light on the Mille Miglia, the 1,000-mile (hence the name) race on Italian roads, from Brescia in the north to Rome and back again. Millions of spectators lined the route to watch their heroes battle for glory at breakneck speeds, sometimes with deadly results.
The Mille, which ran from 1927 to 1957, was (spoiler alert for moviegoers) cancelled after the tragedy of 1957, when the Ferrari 335 S driven by the Marquis de Portago crashed at high speed just 50 kilometres from the finish, killing him, co-driver Edmund Nelson, and nine spectators, including five children.
Twenty years later, however, the Mille Miglia was resurrected as a Storica, a multi-day "regularity" rally for classic and vintage machinery, with participation limited to cars that competed in the original Milles (or are of the same year, make and model). It is on the must-do list for gearheads of sufficient means - the basic entry fee last year was about $17,700 - with a long line of applicants. Participation was capped at 400 cars in 2023.
But while the stars of the show are the mega-pricey Ferraris (classic car insurance provider Hagerty pegs the very rare 335 S of the type Portago drove at $48 million), as well as Maseratis, Alfas, Mercedes, Astons, Jaguars, etc., the variety of cars that can compete are as prosaic as they are prolific. Not only smaller sports cars such as Triumph TR2s and 3s, MGAs, Oscas, and Siatas, but truly basic family-type transportation - think DKWs, Fiat Topolinos, Citroëns, Renault 4Vs and Volkswagens.
While European manufacturers make up the vast majority of entrants, there are a few American-built cars that qualify. At the risk of sounding snooty, most of these Detroit (and South Bend, Ind.,) models are, at first blush, not exactly race material. Still, they are decidedly less expensive, not to mention more comfortable, for those looking for a cheap ride with which to enter.
It would be natural to think the Corvette, which debuted in 1953, is on the list. That would be a no - at least until last year, when a 1957 car appeared as an entry in the Storica. (An error, perhaps, or a new concession by the organizers?) Technically, the Corvette was never entered by General Motors as a factory ride during the Mille Miglia, nor was it a private entry.
However, its crosstown rival from Dearborn, Mich., was in the 1957 Mille. A red 1955 Ford Thunderbird, entered by Italians Francois Smadsa and Anna Rafelli, left the start at 4:34 a.m. (hence wearing the race number 434) and, 15 hours, 11 minutes, 11 seconds later, crossed the finish line in 137th place, not a terrible showing for a heavy (1,525 kilogram) vehicle.
The Thunderbird could be considered a veritable speedster, though, compared to the next two American lead sleds that competed. First up, the 1954 Oldsmobile Holiday Super 88 Coupe, replete with 185-horsepower, 324 cubic inch V8 (with four-barrel carburetor), car No. 334 was entered by the team of Santi and Pozzato (sorry, no first names are recorded) in the '54 race.
Unfortunately, the car did not make it to the finish. Like the T-Bird, the Olds would have been a handful on the narrow roads. To be fair, though, the big (5.1-litre) Rocket 88 engine powering it made the Coupe a force to be reckoned with during the early days of NASCAR.
One year earlier, a 1953 Chrysler Saratoga (1,791 kg) ran in the Mille Miglia piloted by noted Belgian driver/journalist Paul Frère and Andre Milhoux. This was a similar car to the 1951 model that placed 11th in Mexico's famed Carrera Panamericana race. These cars were powered by the first of Chrysler's iconic Hemi engines, introduced in '51. The 331 cu. in. (5.4 L) V8 produced 180 hp and was mated to a four-speed automatic in the Saratoga. (Interestingly, the Hemi engine was formerly named Firepower V-8 by Chrysler). Frère and Milhoux finished 58th overall, and first in the Unlimited Touring class.
I think the sleek Studebaker Golden Hawk, a two-door pillarless hardtop produced between 1956 and '58 was the prettiest of the postwar American production entries in the Mille.
The most powerful as well, with a 275-hp, 352 cu. in. (5.8 L) Packard V8 under its hood. A pair of Swiss motorists registered as Daniel Pache and R. Flury participated in the 1956 race, but retired the car less than 320 km in, the reason for the DNF not recorded.
The Golden Hawk was, in its day, noted for exceptional performance for a production car. The July 1956 issue of Speed Age magazine tested the Golden Hawk against the Chrysler 300B, Ford T-Bird and Chevy Corvette, and found it was faster to 60 miles per hour as well as through the quarter-mile.
Let's also allow for the inclusion of a couple of Anglo-American hybrids, the Nash-Healey and the Allard J2.
Healey produced open-top and coupe versions of the Nash-Healey, powered first by a 235 cu. in. (3. 9L) L-head in-line-six from the Ambassador, later by a larger 253 cu. in. (4.1 L) unit. Donald Healey and his son Geoff competed in 1951 in a Nash-Healey 3850 Spyder to achieve a credible fourth in class and 30th overall.
They returned the following year in a coupe but crashed during the race, Donald Healey retiring from racing after that. However, another Nash-Healey, a Silverstone model, entered by the team of Leslie Johnson and WA McKenzie, finished a surprising fourth in the top class and seventh overall. A special car sporting a Pinin Farina Spider body was prepared for the 1953 Mille Miglia for the American team of John Fitch and Raymond Willday. Reportedly incredibly fast, the car nonetheless retired with brake failure.
During the 1930s, Englishman Sydney Allard, a budding racer who owned a Ford dealership near London, successfully married a Ford flathead V8 retrieved from a wrecked car with a Bugatti body. Its success in local events caught the attention of fellow competitors, who inquired about Allard building similar "specials." About a dozen were produced before the Second World War erupted.
After the end of hostilities, Allard returned to this idea, going into business as a constructor, with three models being offered: the J, the K and the L. Of the three, the J, a cigar-shaped open two-seater, was the one designed for competition. After Allard grenaded his J2's Mercury flathead while competing in the 1951 Giro di Sicili, he towed the car back to London and fitted it with a 331 cu. in. Cadillac V8 (160 hp in stock form) for the Mille Miglia.
For something quite different, turn your attention to legendary American hot rodder Ak Miller. In 1953, Miller, a founder of the National Hot Rod Association (NHRA), decided to enter the Carrera Panamericana in an Oldsmobile-powered Ford Model T-bodied homegrown special named the El Caballo de Hierro (Iron Horse). Competing against some very expensive European machinery in the large sports car class, he and co-driver Doug Harrison finished eighth (14th overall). The following year they finished fifth in class (seventh overall).
When the Carrera was cancelled after the 1955 race, he set his sights on the Mille with a more sophisticated entry. Built for the 1957 race, El Caballo II consisted of a Kurtis Kraft race chassis, an aluminum body and Chrysler Hemi power. Though the sleek sports car made the cover of Hot Rod magazine (April 1957), a solid showing eluded Miller and Harrison, the car retiring early in the race with brake issues.
Should prewar American cars be of interest, there are several that will make the Mille list. In 1928, the second year of the race, a quartet of Chrysler 72s were entered, along with a couple of La Salles, which was an offshoot of the Cadillac brand. The La Salles finished the race in good position - one first in class - as did two of the Chryslers (second and third in class). Chryslers finished first in class in the 1929 and 1930 Mille Miglias.
For the 1931 race, a Graham-Paige was entered by the team by the team of Rognoni and Lorenzetti, finishing 33rd.
What has become one of the world's premier vintage racing events is only a bundle of money, a lot of patience and a car away - as long as it's the right car, the organizing body being a stickler for accuracy and originality. Nonetheless, finding a postwar American-built entry that could be eligible is not as hard as, say, locating a 1950 Stanguellini 1100 Sport. Start looking through websites such as Hemmings, Hagerty, or Bring a Trailer. For a lot less than $67,000 for a car, you, too, could find yourself one of the lucky ones participating in a future edition of what has been called "the most beautiful race in the world." Driving.ca

Subject: Automobile racing

Company / organization: Name: National Hot Rod Association; NAICS: 813910

Document 65 of 108

Interest rates, inflation put homes out of reach for many, summit hears

Author: Shreve, Ellwood

Publication info: Shreve, Ellwood. 

https://libproxy.wlu.ca/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/interest-rates-inflation-put-homes-out-reach-many/docview/2933286584/se-2?accountid=15090

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://ocul-wlu.primo.exlibrisgroup.com/discovery/openurl?institution=01OCUL_WLU&amp;vid=01OCUL_WLU:WLU_DEF&amp;?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Acanadiannews&amp;atitle=Interest+rates%2C+inflation+put+homes+out+of+reach+for+many%2C+summit+hears&amp;title=The+Windsor+Star&amp;issn=&amp;date=2024-02-29&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.2&amp;au=Shreve%2C+Ellwood&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=The+Windsor+Star&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: Home ownership remains out of reach for many people as interest rates, inflation and other issues affect the real estate market, a regional housing summit has heard.
Chatham-Kent Association of Realtors members joined Mayor Darrin Canniff and other area politicians at last Friday's Western Ontario Housing Economic Summit in London, which saw expert panellists discuss a wide range of issues, a release said.
The need for more waste water and other infrastructure spending by Ontario municipalities, particularly for emergency preparedness, was a key message.
Canniff highlighted the substantial financial commitment required.
"In Chatham-Kent, we're facing an estimated $1.5-billion investment over the next decade just for new infrastructure and repairs," he said in a release. "This is indicative of the hundreds of billions of dollars that will be necessary provincewide in the coming years to address waste water challenges."
MPP Matthew Rae Chatham-Kent Mayor Darrin Canniff, back middle, joined a large contingent of Chatham-Kent Association of Realtors at the Western Ontario Housing Economic Summit in London last Friday.
(PC-Perth-Wellington) stressed the importance of proactive investment in waste water infrastructure, particularly upgrading pumping stations to increase capacity.
"Upgrading waste water infrastructure, such as pumping stations, is crucial for accommodating growth," he said in the release. "Investing up front in high-quality infrastructure not only helps expedite housing construction, but also ensures resilience against the impacts of climate change, benefiting future generations."
Canniff and Rae cited waste water infrastructure's key role in sustaining communities and mitigating environmental risks and urged participants to make longterm planning and investment in waste water systems a priority.
Association agent Carson Warrener shed light on significant shifts in Canada's housing market.
The emergence of single-family homes as a national and global financial asset has been a historic milestone in Canadian real estate, he said.
"Recent trends have seen an influx of out-of-town speculators, accounting for up to half of our sales," Warrener said.
"While discussing supply and demand is commonplace, it's crucial to examine the underlying assumptions," he added. "The identity of the end user varies, particularly in smaller communities like Chatham-Kent, where homes were historically undervalued."
Post-pandemic price increases have pushed the average home price to $400,000, but "wage growth has not kept pace, resulting in significant affordability challenges for residents," he said.
He highlighted a unique situation in Chatham-Kent, where some 700 serviced residential building lots remain undeveloped.
"Despite having ample supply, a third of our listings consist of new homes that have been standing vacant," Warrener said.
As the real estate landscape evolves, he stressed the importance of addressing affordability concerns in smaller communities alongside broader supply issues.
"While the housing market grapples with supply challenges, it's imperative to explore strategies to enhance affordability," he said.

Subject: Infrastructure; Interest rates; Economic summit conferences

Title: Interest rates, inflation put homes out of reach for many, summit hears

Publication title: The Windsor Star; Windsor, Ont.

First page: A.2

Publication year: 2024

Publication date: Feb 29, 2024

Section: City + Region

Publisher: Postmedia Network Inc.

Place of publication: Windsor, Ont.

Country of publication: Canada

Publication subject: Business And Economics--Banking And Finance

Document 66 of 108

Deal could add doctors in region

Author: Mazak, Madeline

Publication info: Mazak, Madeline. 

https://libproxy.wlu.ca/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/deal-could-add-doctors-region/docview/2933270440/se-2?accountid=15090

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://ocul-wlu.primo.exlibrisgroup.com/discovery/openurl?institution=01OCUL_WLU&amp;vid=01OCUL_WLU:WLU_DEF&amp;?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Acanadiannews&amp;atitle=Deal+could+add+doctors+in+region&amp;title=The+Windsor+Star&amp;issn=&amp;date=2024-02-29&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=SR.1&amp;au=Mazak%2C+Madeline&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=The+Windsor+Star&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: A 'historic'new $3.1-billion bilateral agreement between Ottawa and Ontario promises to add more local health care workers and reduce emergency room wait times in Windsor-Essex.
The new funds are aimed at reducing the current strain on Ontario's health care system and is being described as the first of several bilateral agreements over the next 10 years that will total $8.4 billion.
The funding will help improve accessibility to family physicians, shrink emergency room wait times and surgery backlogs, and be used to hire more health care workers across the province, the two senior governments say.
"Residents here in Windsor-Essex and across Canada are not getting the health care system that they deserve," said MP Irek Kusmierczyk (L - Windsor-Tecumseh).
"I talk to residents all the time, and they raise health care as one of the top issues.
"We're seeing emergency rooms being overwhelmed, we're seeing people waiting forever for surgeries, and we're also seeing healthcare workers getting burnt out because they're shouldering more and more of the work."
With approximately 30,000 to 40,000 residents in the area not even having a family doctor, "there is an absolute acute shortage of family doctors in Windsor-Essex," Kusmierczyk told the Star.
Provincewide, he added, roughly 2.3 million people are in the same situation.
"The reason this is a big issue is because a lot of folks end up in the ER. The issues that they're coming with are not necessarily emergency issues, they're things that could be treated by a family doctor."
Windsor Regional Hospital reports that 1,876 of its total of 1,972 nursing positions are currently filled.
A recent survey conducted by Unifor Local 2458, which represents roughly 2,500 health care workers in Windsor and Essex County, points to burnout among staff.
Of the 750 Canadian Union of Public Employees workers surveyed, 62 per cent said they were dealing with exhaustion and high stress levels, 41 per cent reported dreading going to work and two in five are considering leaving their jobs.
The new funding is part of the federal government's strategy to inject more than $200 billion into national health care over the next decade.
Within the funding framework, $25 billion is allocated through tailored bilateral agreements with the provinces and territories. The agreement with Ontario is the fifth announced so far, after British Columbia, Prince Edward Island, Alberta and Nova Scotia.
"This is a historic agreement between the province of Ontario and the federal government," said Windsor Regional Hospital spokesperson Steve Erwin.
"Similar to recent significant investments by the Ford government in pediatric emergency department diversion and health human resource recruitment incentives, we will look forward to working with our provincial health partners on strategic investments to advance health care in our region and across Ontario."
The plan is expected to add thousands of health care workers, including family doctors, nurse practitioners, nurses, pharmacists and social workers to the current ranks. It will also open up 700 spots in medical education programs across the province.
In a social media post Thursday, local MPP and Ford government member Andrew Dowie (PC - Windsor-Tecumseh) thanked Ottawa and said the province will continue working with federal officials on "long-term stable funding to ensure Ontarians get the health care they need & deserve."
Ontario's Foreign Credential Recognition Program will also be streamlined to allow more in-ternationally educated health care workers to enter the workforce faster. That program follows the federal government's $86-billion announcement last month to recognize 6,600 internationally educated health care workers.
According to Kusmierczyk, the process required toward professional recognition in the health care fields can cost an individual upward of $10,000.
"We're trying to take some of the cost out of it for the individual doctors, nurses and PSWs," he said, "and work with the province to cut down the time it takes to get recognized, because that's the biggest hurdle."
Erwin highlighted the local hospital's continuing efforts to drive timely patient care and attract more health care professionals to the hospital.
Windsor Regional Hospital recently announced that, bolstered by another funding boost from the Ontario Ministry of Health, it will expand its pediatric emergency division to offer services seven days a week starting March 1.
The local hospital's plan aimed at addressing nursing shortages was also widely successful. In the first year since the Community Commitment Program for Nurses launched in June 2022, more than 200 new nurses signed up.
The new bilateral agreement comes in response to an "unprecedented" number of temporary closings of vital hospital services across Ontario over the past three years.
The Ontario Health Coalition reported that, in 2023, emergency rooms in the province were closed 868 times due to staff shortages. On 316 occasions, an urgent care centre closed its doors.
Essex County Medical Society president Dr. Grant Fortowsky was unavailable for comment ahead of deadline.
Provinces and territories are required to publicly report their progress in meeting targets set by the Canadian Institute of Health Information. mmazak@postmedia.com

Document 67 of 108

Ottawa fires back on 'surprise' student cap; 'Complete garbage,'immigration minister says of complaints that provinces were blindsided

Author: Tumilty, Ryan

Publication info: Tumilty, Ryan. 

https://libproxy.wlu.ca/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/ottawa-fires-back-on-surprise-student-cap/docview/2932703586/se-2?accountid=15090

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://ocul-wlu.primo.exlibrisgroup.com/discovery/openurl?institution=01OCUL_WLU&amp;vid=01OCUL_WLU:WLU_DEF&amp;?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Acanadiannews&amp;atitle=Ottawa+fires+back+on+%27surprise%27+student+cap%3B+%27Complete+garbage%2C%27immigration+minister+says+of+complaints+that+provinces+were+blindsided&amp;title=The+Windsor+Star&amp;issn=&amp;date=2024-02-28&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=NP.1&amp;au=Tumilty%2C+Ryan&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=The+Windsor+Star&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: Immigration Minister Marc Miller said his government gave provinces ample notice that international student numbers would be capped and any suggestion otherwise is "complete garbage."
This after Ontario's College and Universities Minister Jill Dunlop told the London Free Press Monday she was "very disappointed" with what she said was the federal government's "unilateral decision, without any consultation" to limit international students.
"This was dropped on us," Dunlop said.
Miller announced a cap on international student numbers earlier this year.
Universities and colleges across the country have brought in increasing numbers of international students in recent years, rising to nearly 900,000 this year.
On Tuesday, Miller rejected any suggestion provinces weren't fully informed.
"That's complete garbage," he said.
"We said quite clearly they need to get their houses in order. We spoke specifically about Ontario that has the largest number of international students. They should have known it. They've had auditor general reports. We've spoken quite publicly about it."
Miller said his government invited provincial counterparts to meetings that they did not attend.
"It's beneath me to share text messages with journalists, but the reality is that there was communication that just was never followed up on," he told reporters.
International student numbers have tripled over what they were when the Liberals took office in 2015, and the cap lowers them by 35 per cent for next year.
It also spreads out the cuts on a per-capita basis, with Ontario facing the deepest cuts.
On Monday, Dunlop announced nearly $1.3 billion in provincial funding over the next three years for Ontario schools to help them compensate for the lost revenue from fewer international students. International students pay much higher tuition than domestic students and in Ontario domestic tuition fees are frozen, a freeze Dunlop extended for the next three years.
Miller said many international students were landing in Canada only to claim refugee status once here, making it clear they were not seeking out a better education.
"Ontario over the last three years in their education system has had over 10,000 asylum claims. That isn't the sign of a healthy system."
Asylum claims from international students at Seneca College increased from 300 in 2022 to almost 700 in 2023. At Conestoga College, claims jumped from 106 to 450 during that same period.
Miller called those increases "alarming" and "totally unacceptable."
Miller said the federal government is using the only tool it has, reducing the number of permits, but provinces and schools should now be stepping up and taking their own measures.
However, Miller warned that the government is ready to step in and shut down shady schools that are abusing the international student program if provinces don't crack down themselves.
The minister said there are problems across the college sector, but some of the worst offenders are private institutions - and those schools need to go.
"There's responsibility to go around," Miller said on Parliament Hill. "I just think that some of the really, really bad actors are in the private sphere and those need to be shut down."
Provinces are responsible for addressing problems in the post-secondary sector with regards to international students, he said. But if they won't do it, Ottawa will - although "jurisdictional questions" limit the federal government's power.
One potential fix, Miller said, is the federal plan to recognize post-secondary institutions that have higher standards for services, supports and outcomes for international students.
"The recognized institution model that we launched in the fall still is very pertinent to this discussion, because we will be able to separate the wheat from the chaff," he said.
"And perhaps even - if provinces don't assume their responsibility - shut down institutions ourselves if they don't do a good enough job."
National Post, with additional reporting from Canadian Press rtumilty@postmedia.com

Subject: Tuition; Foreign students; Colleges & universities; Immigration

Document 68 of 108

Board trustee seeks advice from police after threats over Kingsville school name

Author: Macleod, Brian

Publication info: Macleod, Brian. 

https://libproxy.wlu.ca/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/board-trustee-seeks-advice-police-after-threats/docview/2932703585/se-2?accountid=15090

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://ocul-wlu.primo.exlibrisgroup.com/discovery/openurl?institution=01OCUL_WLU&amp;vid=01OCUL_WLU:WLU_DEF&amp;?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Acanadiannews&amp;atitle=Board+trustee+seeks+advice+from+police+after+threats+over+Kingsville+school+name&amp;title=The+Windsor+Star&amp;issn=&amp;date=2024-02-28&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.1&amp;au=Macleod%2C+Brian&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=The+Windsor+Star&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: The public school board trustee who introduced the motion to name a new school set to open this fall in Kingsville, Erie Migration Academy, has sought advice from the OPP on personal protection after a deluge of social media criticism and some threats.
Julia Burgess advanced the name of the kindergarten to Grade 12 school at the Feb. 20 meeting of the Greater Essex County District School Board over the two names narrowed down by a public consultation process: Kingsville District Academy and Greater Kingsville Academy, for consideration by trustees.
One of the longlisted names for the school suggested by a member of the public was Lake Erie Migration Academy, but that name was not advanced to the board for consideration.
The board voted 6-2 in favour of Burgess's motion, with trustees Nancy Armstrong and Linda Qin opposing.
But the backlash has been fierce, said Burgess, who chaired the naming committee during the public consultation process that collected about 600 suggestions.
"It's real, and hatred, and really horrible, really horrible," she said.
Qin plans to introduce a motion to reconsider at the next board meeting on March 19.
"There is a lot of dissatisfaction from the parents, from the community," Qin said Tuesday.
Many people are also upset with the last-minute motion that bypassed the two suggested names put before the board, she said.
"I think there is something wrong with the procedure and it won't hurt if we reconsider the name or refer it back to the committee."
Many who oppose Erie Migration Academy want "Kingsville" to be included in the school name. Nevertheless, Burgess stands by the name. And Qin might have a problem submitting a motion to reconsider because board rules say only someone on the prevailing side can introduce such a motion, she said.
Still, if such a motion were successfully introduced, Burgess indicated she might entertain a "narrow" motion to add another word to the school name to avoid an unfortunate acronym indicating a sexual activity. The name's acronym, described in the online urbandictionary.com, is sometimes used by young people, she said.
Burgess also took issue with comments made by Kingsville Mayor Dennis Rogers at a recent council meeting.
"We've seen the social media comments, we've seen emails and messages and you know for people in the community, we're very unhappy with the name chosen for the new Kingsville school," Rogers said in an online video of his comments. He noted a 2,000-word petition launched by the students and he lauded their willingness to voice their concerns, including at a peaceful protest in front of the school held on Feb. 23.
"Can you imagine the confidence in pride that this would instil in our students ... to get this victory? Can you imagine the faith that this would instil in our education leaders for them to hear these voices and reconsider? That is how we all will win," he said.
Burgess objected to the "zero-sum" approach with the use of the word "victory," indicating that "somebody must win, somebody must lose."
In an email to The Star Monday, Rogers advised the board to listen to students.
"I can't even tell you how proud I am of our students who stood up for what they believe in," Rogers said in the email. "Their voices have been heard from Kingsville all the way to Queen's Park. I hope the school board hears them too."
MPP Anthony Leardi (PC-Essex) told the school board in a Feb. 24 letter it has "made misstep with the proposed name" of the new Kingsville school.
Leardi wrote the name "means nothing to no one."
"At the same time, the board seems to be sending the message that somehow the name 'Kingsville'is inappropriate. Yet, no one has enunciated why," he wrote.
Including Kingsville in the school name is "appropriate and in good taste," he wrote. "It also has the added value of fitting in with at least (four) of the board's (five) naming criteria," Leardi wrote."
The board should simply reconsider the choice and follow the process again."
In an interview with The Star Feb. 26, Leardi said the decision to write the letter came after he received input from the community.
"It's a community school, and the community feels, I think correctly, that the name of the school should reflect the general consensus of the community, and I happen to agree with that," he said.
Burgess, in a letter of response to Leardi Feb. 26, argued the name Erie Migration Academy adheres to all five of the naming criteria and that the board was obliged to choose a name that represents the school's part in the district. bamacleod@postmedia.com

Subject: Names; Students; Social networks; School boards

Business indexing term: Subject: Social networks

Identifier / keyword: public; school; board; trustee; introduced; motion

During the Feb. 20 board meeting, Burgess and board chair Gale Hatfield noted the board's regulations say "trustees can vote to choose one of the suggestions from the committee or provide an alternative." Burgess then noted that migration routes - avian and human - are significant to Kingsville, that the catchment area includes many surrounding communities and that "academy" is already well-established in the board. But opponents quickly rallied against the name, complaining it should include the word Kingsville and criticizing the process that was used to select the name. A post on one Kingsville social media site is asking people to complain to the Ontario ombudsman. Another protest is planned for March 1. bamacleod@postmedia.com

Document 70 of 108

Poilievre blames Liberal 'turf war' for city losing federal funding; Tory slams Ottawa after Windsor was rejected for millions in housing support

Author: Mazak, Madeline

Publication info: Mazak, Madeline. 

https://libproxy.wlu.ca/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/poilievre-blames-liberal-turf-war-city-losing/docview/2931243101/se-2?accountid=15090

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://ocul-wlu.primo.exlibrisgroup.com/discovery/openurl?institution=01OCUL_WLU&amp;vid=01OCUL_WLU:WLU_DEF&amp;?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Acanadiannews&amp;atitle=Poilievre+blames+Liberal+%27turf+war%27+for+city+losing+federal+funding%3B+Tory+slams+Ottawa+after+Windsor+was+rejected+for+millions+in+housing+support&amp;title=The+Windsor+Star&amp;issn=&amp;date=2024-02-24&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.2&amp;au=Mazak%2C+Madeline&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=The+Windsor+Star&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: In a visit to Windsor on Friday, Opposition leader Pierre Poilievre slammed the Liberal government's recent rejection of Windsor's application to access millions in federal housing subsidies.
"Trudeau is getting into bureaucratic and political turf wars with cities he doesn't like and worsening the problem," the Conservative Party leader told the Windsor Star.
Pointing to the cost of living having nearly doubled during eight years of Liberal leadership, Poilievre said federal housing grants should not hinge on government-prescribed zoning requirements, but rather be pegged to actual building progress.
Poilievre's visit to Windsor came at the conclusion of a three-day swing through southwestern Ontario. During a one-on-one interview with the Star, he offered his thoughts on the Liberal government's multibillion-dollar Housing Accelerator Fund, designed to help address Canada's housing crisis, as well as on his call for an inquiry into federal subsidies for Windsor's NextStar Energy battery facility.
Federal Housing Minister Sean Fraser last month advised Windsor that its Housing Accelerator Fund application had been rejected, stating in an email to Windsor Mayor Drew Dilkens that only "ambitious" communities would receive funding.
Unlike with successful applicants, including many other Ontario cities, Windsor refused to amend its zoning bylaw to permit fourplexes on any residential lot. Triplexes as-of-right are currently mandated by the province, which Windsor did not oppose.
Windsor had been qualified for up to $30 million in federal housing grants, and potentially millions more if targets were met.
Rather than tying senior government grants to municipal zoning regulation amendments, Poilievre said a Conservative federal government would focus on actual builds.
"My common sense plan is to pay the money out based on results, rather than prescribing exactly how cities should get homes built," said Poilievre. "Why not just pay for getting them done?" Poilievre said his party's plan would require municipalities to increase housing construction by 15 per cent annually in order to qualify for funding.
"They will be paid like realtors and builders," he said. "That way we don't have to get into these turf wars over who makes these decisions over zoning or bylaws and setbacks, and simply just pay for the result."
When it comes to Windsor's NextStar Energy, Poilievre told the Star his party is still pushing for answers about how many taxpayer-funded jobs are going to temporary foreign workers.
Last fall, Poilievre pointed to foreign workers being brought in during construction of the multibillion-dollar facility and called for a public inquiry. He also demanded that the terms of the agreement - the federal and provincial governments have committed up to $15 billion in production incentives, if targets are met - be publicly disclosed.
In response, NextStar Energy CEO Danies Lee told the Star in November that the workers being brought in from Japan and South Korea are only here temporarily and needed to set up specialized machinery in a new industry.
Both the federal and provincial governments said the thousands of workers needed to construct the giant facility, and to operate it once completed, would be locally sourced.
Poilievre said he still wants answers.
"Whenever we try to pursue accountability in the parliamentary committees, the NDPs and Liberals team up and vote down any of our inquiries," he said. "We're calling on the NDP to start working for workers, and stop working for Justin Trudeau."
Poilievre said he feels that the Liberal and NDP parties are "covering up the truth.
"They don't want people to know how many tax dollars we're spending on foreign workers from South Korea. My common sense plan is that no tax dollars should go to subsidize foreign workers.
"Every single tax dollar for economic development must go exclusively to our people in this country."
As for the long-awaited Gordie Howe International Bridge, Poilievre condemned the Liberal government for the project going over budget.
In January, the groups responsible for the construction of the new international border crossing said the opening will be pushed back nearly a year - with an extra $700 million added to the price tag. Canada is funding the $6.4-billion project, with bridge tolls expected to eventually cover that cost.
"We need a common sense Conservative government that does fixed-price contracts with the builders so they have to deliver on time and on budget," said Poilievre.
"If they don't, then they pay the price.
"We built a massive bridge over the Rideau River in Ottawa while I was minister," he said, referring to his cabinet role in the former Harper Conservative government.
"The company went bankrupt.
But they were insured to pay the full price of delivering the project at no extra cost to the taxpayer," he said.
"That's how this stuff should work."
Continuing to deliver on his "common sense" message in Windsor on Friday, Poilievre met with members of the Community Support Centre of Essex County, and talked with workers and others at CenterLine, Cavalier Tool & Manufacturing Ltd., and Ground Effects.
Also on the agenda was a meeting with a carpenters union at a Rosati Group construction site, and with members of the Ontario Federation of Anglers and Hunters to discuss a Liberal/NDP plan on regulating firearms. mmazak@postmedia.com

Document 71 of 108

Poilievre blames Liberal 'turf war' for Windsor losing millions in federal housing funds

Author: Mazak, Madel ine

Publication info: Mazak, Madeline. 

https://libproxy.wlu.ca/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/blogs-podcasts-websites/poilievre-blames-liberal-turf-war-windsor-losing/docview/2931156836/se-2?accountid=15090

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://ocul-wlu.primo.exlibrisgroup.com/discovery/openurl?institution=01OCUL_WLU&amp;vid=01OCUL_WLU:WLU_DEF&amp;?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Acanadiannews&amp;atitle=Poilievre+blames+Liberal+%27turf+war%27+for+Windsor+losing+millions+in+federal+housing+funds&amp;title=The+Windsor+Star+%28Online%29&amp;issn=&amp;date=2024-02-23&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=&amp;au=Mazak%2C+Madeline&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=The+Windsor+Star+%28Online%29&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: In a visit to Windsor on Friday, Opposition leader Pierre Poilievre slammed the Liberal government's recent rejection of Windsor's application to access millions in federal housing subsidies.
"Trudeau is getting into bureaucratic and political turf wars with cities he doesn't like and worsening the problem," the Conservative Party leader told the Windsor Star.
Pointing to the cost of living having nearly doubled during eight years of Liberal leadership, Poilievre said federal housing grants should not hinge on government-prescribed zoning requirements, but rather be pegged to actual building progress.
Poilievre's visit to Windsor came at the conclusion of a three-day swing through southwestern Ontario. During a one-on-one interview with the Star, he offered his thoughts on the Liberal government's multibillion-dollar Housing Accelerator Fund, designed to help address Canada's housing crisis, as well as on his call for an inquiry into federal subsidies for Windsor's NextStar Energy battery facility.
Federal Housing Minister Sean Fraser last month advised Windsor that its Housing Accelerator Fund application had been rejected, stating in an email to Windsor Mayor Drew Dilkens that only "ambitious" communities would receive funding.
Unlike with successful applicants, including many other Ontario cities, Windsor refused to amend its zoning bylaw to permit fourplexes on any residential lot. Triplexes as-of-right are currently mandated by the province, which Windsor did not oppose.
Windsor had been qualified for up to $30 million in federal housing grants, and potentially millions more if targets were met.
Rather than tying senior government grants to municipal zoning regulation amendments, Poilievre said a Conservative federal government would focus on actual builds.
"My common sense plan is to pay the money out based on results, rather than prescribing exactly how cities should get homes built," said Poilievre. "Why not just pay for getting them done?"
Poilievre said his party's plan would require municipalities to increase housing construction by 15 per cent annually in order to qualify for funding.
"They will be paid like realtors and builders," he said. "That way we don't have to get into these turf wars over who makes these decisions over zoning or bylaws and setbacks, and simply just pay for the result."
Federal Conservative Leader Pierre Poilievre speaks to employees at CenterLine Limited in Windsor on Friday, Feb. 23, 2024.
When it comes to Windsor's NextStar Energy, Poilievre told the Star his party is still pushing for answers about how many taxpayer-funded jobs are going to temporary foreign workers.
Last fall, Poilievre pointed to foreign workers being brought in during construction of the multibillion-dollar facility and called for a public inquiry. He also demanded that the terms of the agreement - the federal and provincial governments have committed up to $15 billion in production incentives, if targets are met - be publicly disclosed.
In response, NextStar Energy CEO Danies Lee told the Star in November that the workers being brought in from Japan and South Korea are only here temporarily and needed to set up specialized machinery in a new industry.
Both the federal and provincial governments said the thousands of workers needed to construct the giant facility, and to operate it once completed, would be locally sourced.
Poilievre said he still wants answers.
"Whenever we try to pursue accountability in the parliamentary committees, the NDPs and Liberals team up and vote down any of our inquiries," he said. "We're calling on the NDP to start working for workers, and stop working for Justin Trudeau."
Poilievre said he feels that the Liberal and NDP parties are "covering up the truth.
"They don't want people to know how many tax dollars we're spending on foreign workers from South Korea. My common sense plan is that no tax dollars should go to subsidize foreign workers.
"Every single tax dollar for economic development must go exclusively to our people in this country."
Michael Beneteau, left, CEO of CenterLine Limited in Windsor, speaks with federal Conservative Leader Pierre Poilievre during a tour of the facility on Friday.
As for the long-awaited Gordie Howe International Bridge, Poilievre condemned the Liberal government for the project going over budget.
In January, the groups responsible for the construction of the new international border crossing said the opening will be pushed back nearly a year - with an extra $700 million added to the price tag. Canada is funding the $6.4-billion project, with bridge tolls expected to eventually cover that cost.
"We need a common sense conservative government that does fixed-price contracts with the builders so they have to deliver on time and on budget," said Poilievre.
"If they don't, then they pay the price.
"We built a massive bridge over the Rideau River in Ottawa while I was minister," he said, referring to his cabinet role in the former Harper Conservative government.
"The company went bankrupt. But they were insured to pay the full price of delivering the project at no extra cost to the taxpayer," he said.
"That's how this stuff should work."

Also on the agenda was a meeting with a carpenters union at a Rosati Group construction site, and with members of the Ontario Federation of Anglers and Hunters to discuss a Liberal/NDP plan on regulating firearms.

Document 72 of 108

Deal could add doctors in region; Local officials anxious to see how area benefits from $3.1B agreement

Author: Mazak, Madeline

Publication info: Mazak, Madeline. 

https://libproxy.wlu.ca/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/deal-could-add-doctors-region-local-officials/docview/2931067630/se-2?accountid=15090

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://ocul-wlu.primo.exlibrisgroup.com/discovery/openurl?institution=01OCUL_WLU&amp;vid=01OCUL_WLU:WLU_DEF&amp;?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Acanadiannews&amp;atitle=Deal+could+add+doctors+in+region%3B+Local+officials+anxious+to+see+how+area+benefits+from+%243.1B+agreement&amp;title=The+Windsor+Star&amp;issn=&amp;date=2024-02-23&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.1&amp;au=Mazak%2C+Madeline&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=The+Windsor+Star&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: A 'historic'new $3.1-billion bilateral agreement between Ottawa and Ontario promises to add more local health care workers and reduce emergency room wait times in Windsor-Essex.
The new funds are aimed at reducing the current strain on Ontario's health care system and is being described as the first of several bilateral agreements over the next 10 years that will total $8.4 billion.
The funding will help improve accessibility to family physicians, shrink emergency room wait times and surgery backlogs, and be used to hire more health care workers across the province, the two senior governments say.
"Residents here in Windsor-Essex and across Canada are not getting the health care system that they deserve," said MP Irek Kusmierczyk (L - Windsor-Tecumseh).
"I talk to residents all the time, and they raise health care as one of the top issues.
"We're seeing emergency rooms being overwhelmed, we're seeing people waiting forever for surgeries, and we're also seeing healthcare workers getting burnt out because they're shouldering more and more of the work."
With approximately 30,000 to 40,000 residents in the area not even having a family doctor, "there is an absolute acute shortage of family doctors in Windsor-Essex," Kusmierczyk told the Star.
Provincewide, he added, roughly 2.3 million people are in the same situation.
"The reason this is a big issue is because a lot of folks end up in the ER. The issues that they're coming with are not necessarily emergency issues, they're things that could be treated by a family doctor."
Windsor Regional Hospital reports that 1,876 of its total of 1,972 nursing positions are currently filled.
A recent survey conducted by Unifor Local 2458, which represents roughly 2,500 health care workers in Windsor and Essex County, points to burnout among staff.
Of the 750 Canadian Union of Public Employees workers surveyed, 62 per cent said they were dealing with exhaustion and high stress levels, 41 per cent reported dreading going to work and two in five are considering leaving their jobs.
The new funding is part of the federal government's strategy to inject more than $200 billion into national health care over the next decade.
Within the funding framework, $25 billion is allocated through tailored bilateral agreements with the provinces and territories. The agreement with Ontario is the fifth announced so far, after British Columbia, Prince Edward Island, Alberta and Nova Scotia.
"This is a historic agreement between the province of Ontario and the federal government," said Windsor Regional Hospital spokesperson Steve Erwin.
"Similar to recent significant investments by the Ford government in pediatric emergency department diversion and health human resource recruitment incentives, we will look forward to working with our provincial health partners on strategic investments to advance health care in our region and across Ontario."
The plan is expected to add thousands of health care workers, including family doctors, nurse practitioners, nurses, pharmacists and social workers to the current ranks. It will also open up 700 spots in medical education programs across the province.
In a social media post Thursday, local MPP and Ford government member Andrew Dowie (PC - Windsor-Tecumseh) thanked Ottawa and said the province will continue working with federal officials on "long-term stable funding to ensure Ontarians get the health care they need & deserve."
Ontario's Foreign Credential Recognition Program will also be streamlined to allow more internationally educated health care workers to enter the workforce faster. That program follows the federal government's $86-billion announcement last month to recognize 6,600 internationally educated health care workers.
According to Kusmierczyk, the process required toward professional recognition in the health care fields can cost an individual upward of $10,000.
"We're trying to take some of the cost out of it for the individual doctors, nurses and PSWs," he said, "and work with the province to cut down the time it takes to get recognized, because that's the biggest hurdle."
Erwin highlighted the local hospital's continuing efforts to drive timely patient care and attract more health care professionals to the hospital.
Windsor Regional Hospital recently announced that, bolstered by another funding boost from the Ontario Ministry of Health, it will expand its pediatric emergency division to offer services seven days a week starting March 1.
The local hospital's plan aimed at addressing nursing shortages was also widely successful. In the first year since the Community Commitment Program for Nurses launched in June 2022, more than 200 new nurses signed up.
The new bilateral agreement comes in response to an "unprecedented" number of temporary closings of vital hospital services across Ontario over the past three years.
The Ontario Health Coalition reported that, in 2023, emergency rooms in the province were closed 868 times due to staff shortages. On 316 occasions, an urgent care centre closed its doors.
Essex County Medical Society president Dr. Grant Fortowsky was unavailable for comment ahead of deadline.
Provinces and territories are required to publicly report their progress in meeting targets set by the Canadian Institute of Health Information. mmazak@postmedia.com

Subject: Agreements; Hospitals; Emergency medical care; Funding; Pediatrics; Nurses; Medical personnel; Physicians
