Document 91 of 108

Funding for women's shelter urged; Stiles demands funding, declaration intimate partner violence is epidemic

Author: Campbell, Taylor

Publication info: Campbell, Taylor. 

https://libproxy.wlu.ca/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/funding-womens-shelter-urged-stiles-demands/docview/2920910665/se-2?accountid=15090

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://ocul-wlu.primo.exlibrisgroup.com/discovery/openurl?institution=01OCUL_WLU&amp;vid=01OCUL_WLU:WLU_DEF&amp;?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Acanadiannews&amp;atitle=Funding+for+women%27s+shelter+urged%3B+Stiles+demands+funding%2C+declaration+intimate+partner+violence+is+epidemic&amp;title=The+Windsor+Star&amp;issn=&amp;date=2024-02-01&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=SR.1&amp;au=Campbell%2C+Taylor&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=The+Windsor+Star&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: The leader of Ontario's NDP called on the Doug Ford Conservatives to better fund women's shelters and to finally declare intimate partner violence an epidemic during a visit to Windsor this week.
Standing inside Hiatus House following a tour of the facility on Thursday, Marit Stiles said the need for support for those fleeing abuse has risen across the province, but funding hasn't kept up. The government, she said, provides women's shelters "only a fraction" of the money required to address "urgent" repairs and improvements.
"They need to start taking this seriously," she said.
"Our provincial government can no longer turn a blind eye to the struggles that are faced by survivors seeking refuge."
Neither MPP Andrew Dowie (PC - Windsor-Tecumseh) nor MPP Anthony Leardi (PC - Essex) could be reached for comment ahead of the Star's Thursday print deadline.
Declaring intimate partner violence an epidemic is the first recommendation from a coroner's inquest into the deaths of three women murdered by the same ex-partner in Renfrew County more than eight years ago. That inquest resulted in 86 recommendations for action put forward by the coroner's jury in June 2022.
The provincial government has so far refused to declare intimate partner violence an epidemic. In June 2023, the government argued the term epidemic is reserved for the spread of disease.
"If we can't recognize it, if we can't accept it and name it for what it is, then there will be women and children all around the province that will go without the supports and services that they need," said Windsor West NDP MPP Lisa Gretzky.
"For me, the very first, most important step is for the government to call it what it is, like municipalities have bravely done all around the province."
Windsor city council voted unanimously last July to declare intimate partner violence an epidemic following advocacy by the Violence Against Women Co-ordinating Committee Windsor-Essex, a group that represents nearly 30 local organizations that support women experiencing violence and their children.
Hiatus House only receives enough funding to house women in crisis for six to eight weeks, said Maggie Durocher, co-ordinator of fundraising and development for the organization.
"This is an emergency shelter.
After eight weeks, they have to move on. We're releasing them to homelessness. That's the bottom line," Durocher said.
"They're leaving that violent partner, which is what we encourage them to do, but then, after eight weeks, what's next? Where do they go?" Hiatus House is still looking to build 42 transitional housing units on property across the street from its Louis Avenue location. The project is expected to cost $21 million, more than half of which the organization wants governments to fund.
The organization hopes to fundraise the remaining $10 million through private sector donations.
Lady Laforet, executive director of the Welcome Centre Shelter - a Windsor homeless shelter for women and families - said the "intertwining" of homelessness and gender-based violence is "well-known," and that transitional housing can help prevent homelessness.
"While we've been talking about it for decades, what we've actually seen is no purpose-built social housing going up, no more than nominal changes to income support programs," Laforet said.
She also charged Windsor city council with effectively turning down millions in federal housing dollars through the Housing Accelerator Fund due to "short-sighted" NIMBY-ism - money that could be used to create affordable housing.
"All of that ends up being carried largely on the backs of women, women living below the poverty line, women experiencing gender-based violence and intimate partner violence, and resulting, unfortunately, in accessing an emergency shelter system that is woefully underfunded and under-resourced." tcampbell@postmedia.com

Subject: Police reports; Gender-based violence; Domestic violence; Women; Shelters; Funding; Homeless people

Beer Store expands delivery; In brief

https://libproxy.wlu.ca/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/beer-store-expands-delivery-brief/docview/2918910932/se-2?accountid=15090

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://ocul-wlu.primo.exlibrisgroup.com/discovery/openurl?institution=01OCUL_WLU&amp;vid=01OCUL_WLU:WLU_DEF&amp;?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Acanadiannews&amp;atitle=Beer+Store+expands+delivery%3B+In+brief&amp;title=The+Windsor+Star&amp;issn=&amp;date=2024-01-27&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.8&amp;au=&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=The+Windsor+Star&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: The Beer Store is expanding its delivery partnership with DoorDash Canada. The Ontario alcohol retailer says its agreement with the San Francisco-based courier company will enable deliveries from hundreds of stores. The pair initially began making deliveries from 50 stores throughout December, but recently added about 230 more. The Beer Store says couriers using DoorDash to ferry their products have their Smart Serve licences and must ensure deliveries are only made to customers 19 years and older. These couriers cancel alcohol deliveries if the recipient fails to produce a valid ID, appears intoxicated or attempts to purchase for a minor. The Beer Store is owned by Ontario brewers, but its hold on the market has been threatened in recent months by Premier Doug Ford, who announced in December that sales of beer, wine, cider and ready-to-drink cocktails will be allowed in convenience stores and all grocery stores in the province by 2026.

NDP makes plea for women's shelters; Stiles demands funding, declaration intimate partner violence is epidemic

Author: Campbell, Taylor

Document 94 of 108

Ontario NDP leader demands more support for women's shelters during Windsor visit

Author: Campbell, Taylor

Publication info: Campbell, Taylor. 

https://libproxy.wlu.ca/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/blogs-podcasts-websites/ontario-ndp-leader-demands-more-support-womens/docview/2918561965/se-2?accountid=15090

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://ocul-wlu.primo.exlibrisgroup.com/discovery/openurl?institution=01OCUL_WLU&amp;vid=01OCUL_WLU:WLU_DEF&amp;?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Acanadiannews&amp;atitle=Ontario+NDP+leader+demands+more+support+for+women%27s+shelters+during+Windsor+visit&amp;title=The+Windsor+Star+%28Online%29&amp;issn=&amp;date=2024-01-25&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=&amp;au=Campbell%2C+Taylor&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=The+Windsor+Star+%28Online%29&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: The leader of Ontario's NDP called on the Doug Ford Conservatives to better fund women's shelters and to finally declare intimate partner violence an epidemic during a visit to Windsor this week.
Standing inside Hiatus House following a tour of the facility on Thursday, Marit Stiles said the need for support for those fleeing abuse has risen across the province, but funding hasn't kept up.
The government, she said, provides women's shelters "only a fraction" of the money required to address "urgent" repairs and improvements.
"They need to start taking this seriously," she said.
"Our provincial government can no longer turn a blind eye to the struggles that are faced by survivors seeking refuge."
Neither MPP Andrew Dowie (PC - Windsor-Tecumseh) nor MPP Anthony Leardi (PC - Essex) could be reached for comment ahead of the Star's Thursday print deadline.
Declaring intimate partner violence an epidemic is the first recommendation from a coroner's inquest into the deaths of three women murdered by the same ex-partner in Renfrew County more than eight years ago. That inquest resulted in 86 recommendations for action put forward by the coroner's jury in June 2022.
The provincial government has so far refused to declare intimate partner violence an epidemic. In June 2023, the government argued the term epidemic is reserved for the spread of disease.
"If we can't recognize it, if we can't accept it and name it for what it is, then there will be women and children all around the province that will go without the supports and services that they need," said Windsor West NDP MPP Lisa Gretzky.
"For me, the very first, most important step is for the government to call it what it is, like municipalities have bravely done all around the province."
Windsor city council, joining a growing number of Ontario municipalities, voted unanimously last July to declare intimate partner violence an epidemic following advocacy by the Violence Against Women Co-ordinating Committee Windsor-Essex, a group that represents nearly 30 local organizations that support women experiencing violence and their children.
Hiatus House only receives enough funding to house women in crisis for six to eight weeks, said Maggie Durocher, co-ordinator of fundraising and development for the organization.
"This is an emergency shelter. After eight weeks, they have to move on. We're releasing them to homelessness. That's the bottom line," Durocher said.
"They're leaving that violent partner, which is what we encourage them to do, but then, after eight weeks, what's next? Where do they go?"
Erika Broadbent, left, manager of clinical services at Hiatus House in Windsor, gives Ontario NDP Leader Marit Stiles a tour of the facility on Thursday, January 25, 2024.
Hiatus House is still looking to build 42 transitional housing units on property across the street from its Louis Avenue location. The project is expected to cost $21 million, more than half of which the organization wants governments to fund.
The organization hopes to fundraise the remaining $10 million through private sector donations.
Related
Windsor praised for declaring intimate partner violence a local 'epidemic'
Advocates say latest Windsor homicide part of growing femicide 'epidemic'
Lady Laforet, executive director of the Welcome Centre Shelter - a Windsor homeless shelter for women and families - said the "intertwining" of homelessness and gender-based violence is "well-known," and that transitional housing can help prevent homelessness.
"While we've been talking about it for decades, what we've actually seen is no purpose-built social housing going up, no more than nominal changes to income support programs," Laforet said.
She also charged Windsor city council with effectively turning down millions in federal housing dollars through the Housing Accelerator Fund due to "short-sighted" NIMBY-ism - money that could be used to create affordable housing.
"All of that ends up being carried largely on the backs of women, women living below the poverty line, women experiencing gender-based violence and intimate partner violence, and resulting, unfortunately, in accessing an emergency shelter system that is woefully underfunded and under-resourced."
tcampbell@postmedia.com( tcampbell@postmedia.com )
twitter.com/wstarcampbell
MPP Lisa Gretzky (NDP - Windsor West), left, Lady Laforet, executive director of the Welcome Centre Shelter, and Ontario NDP Leader Marit Stiles are shown during a press conference on Thursday, January 25, 2024 at Hiatus House in Windsor.

Document 95 of 108

'The decision ... was not justified'; Govt's use of emergencies act to banish truck convoy violated charter, judge RULES

Author: Nardi, Christopher

Publication info: Nardi, Christopher. 

https://libproxy.wlu.ca/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/decision-was-not-justified-govts-use-emergencies/docview/2918035724/se-2?accountid=15090

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://ocul-wlu.primo.exlibrisgroup.com/discovery/openurl?institution=01OCUL_WLU&amp;vid=01OCUL_WLU:WLU_DEF&amp;?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Acanadiannews&amp;atitle=%27The+decision+...+was+not+justified%27%3B+Govt%27s+use+of+emergencies+act+to+banish+truck+convoy+violated+charter%2C+judge+RULES&amp;title=The+Windsor+Star&amp;issn=&amp;date=2024-01-24&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=NP.1&amp;au=Nardi%2C+Christopher&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=The+Windsor+Star&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: The Liberal government's decision to invoke the Emergencies Act in response to the 2022 anti-mandate convoy protests was unreasonable, unjustified and violated the Charter, the Federal Court has ruled.
In a lengthy ruling published Tuesday, Federal Court Justice Richard Mosley found that though the protests in early 2022 were causing harm to Canada's economy, trade and commerce, they did not rise to the level of a threat to national security as defined by the law.
He thus sided with civil liberties groups who argued that the Libera lgovernment went beyond its powers and was not justified in its historic invocation of the Emergencies Act in February 2022.
"I have concluded that the decision to issue the Proclamation (of the Emergencies Act) does not bear the hallmarks of reasonableness - justification, transparency and intelligibility - and was not justified in relation to the relevant factual and legal constraints that were required to be taken into consideration," Mosley wrote.
On the same day, Deputy Prime Minister Chrystia Freeland said the government did not agree with Mosley's decision and promised to appeal it.
"The public safety of Canadians was under threat, our national security, which includes our national economic security, was under threat," she said.
"I was convinced at the time. It was the right thing to do. It was the necessary thing to do."
Mosley's ruling stems from four legal challenges by civil liberty groups, including the Canadian Civil Liberties Association (CCLA) and the Canadian Constitutional Foundation (CCF).
The groups argued that the Liberals exceeded their powers when they invoked the unprecedented powers of the act on Feb. 14, 2022, in response to ongoing convoy blockades in Ontario and Alberta.
Displaying an uncommon level of introspection in a ruling, Mosley admitted that at the outset of the hearing, his view was "leaning" towards the belief that the government's invocation of the Act was reasonable.
He said he considered the events of the convoy to have gone "beyond legitimate protest" and were an "unacceptable breakdown of public order." He also admitted that had he been at the government's table at the time of the decision, he may have agreed it was necessary to invoke the act.
"I acknowledge that in conducting judicial review of that decision, I am revisiting that time with the benefit of hindsight and a more extensive record of the facts and law than that which was before the (government)," he wrote.
But months of time to "carefully deliberate" on the evidence and arguments, as well as arguments by the CCF and CCLA, changed his mind.
"Their participation in these proceedings has demonstrated again the value of public interest litigants. Especially in presenting informed legal argument. This case may not have turned out the way it has without their involvement," he wrote.
Conser vative Leader Pierre Poilievre also reacted to the news on X (formerly Twitter) by placing the blame solely on Trudeau.
"He caused the crisis by dividing people. Then he violated Charter rights to illegally suppress citizens. As PM, I will unite our country for freedom," he wrote.
National Post

Subject: Charters; National security; Judicial reviews; Civil liberties

Document 96 of 108

Masonic Temple marks over 100 years

Author: Mazak, Madeline

Publication info: Mazak, Madeline. 

https://libproxy.wlu.ca/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/masonic-temple-marks-over-100-years/docview/2917710336/se-2?accountid=15090

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://ocul-wlu.primo.exlibrisgroup.com/discovery/openurl?institution=01OCUL_WLU&amp;vid=01OCUL_WLU:WLU_DEF&amp;?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Acanadiannews&amp;atitle=Masonic+Temple+marks+over+100+years&amp;title=The+Windsor+Star&amp;issn=&amp;date=2024-01-23&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.2&amp;au=Mazak%2C+Madeline&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=The+Windsor+Star&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: One of Windsor's historic downtown landmarks opened its doors to the public on Saturday to celebrate its centenary following a series of renovations to the 100-yearold structure.
Established in 1922, Masonic Temple at the corner of Ouellette Avenue and Erie Street - a longtime regional home to Freemasons - celebrated the milestone at last after festivities were postponed during the COVID-19 pandemic.
The open house welcomed the public to tour the many alcoves of the heritage building while enjoying poetry, dance and musical performances.
"To me this building is a home," said Masonic Temple committee chair and member Cameron Adamson.
"It's as much a community home as much as it is a Masonic home. Especially in 2024, it feels like so many heritage community spaces aren't available like they used to be. Having people work on having it here for another 100 years is a signal that community is part of what we are and part of what we do."
Rehabilitating the structure's facade was a major component of the restoration projects led by Felix Culpa Architecture and Sealcon Ltd.
Work was also carried out inside the building, including modernizing the elevator, automation of all doors, and improvements to security systems.
"We tried to focus on four areas," said Adamson.
"Making the temple as accessible as possible, making it as energy efficient as possible, extending its useful life and preserving the heritage features."
Groups from Michigan and Toronto attended the event, as well as Windsor downtown Ward 3 Coun. Renaldo Agostino, Windsor-Tecumseh PC MPP Andrew Dowie and Windsor West NDP MP Brian Masse.
The Windsor Spitfires were also on hand to present a jersey to Thomas Hogeboom, Grand Master of the Grand Lodge of Canada in the Province of Ontario.
"This is life pumped back into masonry in this community," said Hogeboom.
Hogeboom said freemasonry is witnessing an "upward boom" following the pandemic.
"If the last one hundred years are any indication," said Hogeboom, "I see nothing but great things. I'm sure it (Masonic Temple) will continue to be maintained and supported like it is today."
Funding was provided by the City of Windsor development and heritage standing committee, the Department of Canadian Heritage, and other local supporters.
Adamson said they are working to secure additional funding for ongoing projects.
"There's always going to be something to improve," said Adamson.
"That's something we talk about a lot in Freemasonry. Your work is never done, whether it's working on yourself or working on the building. You're always trying your best to improve it." mmazak@postmedia.com

Document 97 of 108

'No government could afford it'; Why a newly proposed guaranteed basic income is likely doomed

Author: Lévesque, Carine

Publication info: Lévesque, Carine. 

https://libproxy.wlu.ca/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/no-government-could-afford-why-newly-proposed/docview/2917710257/se-2?accountid=15090

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://ocul-wlu.primo.exlibrisgroup.com/discovery/openurl?institution=01OCUL_WLU&amp;vid=01OCUL_WLU:WLU_DEF&amp;?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Acanadiannews&amp;atitle=%27No+government+could+afford+it%27%3B+Why+a+newly+proposed+guaranteed+basic+income+is+likely+doomed&amp;title=The+Windsor+Star&amp;issn=&amp;date=2024-01-23&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=NP.1&amp;au=L%C3%A9vesque%2C+Carine&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=The+Windsor+Star&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: The controversial idea of a guaranteed basic income, which could cost tens of billions of dollars each year, might be making its way to the House of Commons this spring.
But the chances of a guaranteed income becoming actual policy are "very close to zero," said Kevin Milligan, an economics professor at the University of British Columbia who specializes in government social spending.
"No government could afford it. Any government who was wise would see that this is not a good way to work on the important issues of poverty," Milligan said.
The Senate has been studying Ontario Sen. Kim Pate's Bill S-233, which strives to develop a national framework to provide people over the age of 17 in Canada with access to a "guaranteed livable basic income," including temporary workers, permanent residents and refugee claimants.
The bill would not actually implement a guaranteed basic income (GBI), but would force the finance minister to consult with provinces and territories, as well as Indigenous and other groups, to prepare a report setting out a framework for the policy.
If it passes a final vote in the Senate, the bill will head to the House of Commons. Liberals and Conservatives are currently refusing to take a clear position on the bill, arguing that they are waiting for the legislation to reach the lower chamber before they decide where they stand.
That sparked speculation in the following days from right-wing commentators such as the Western Standard's Cory Morgan and the Post Millennial's David Krayden, and also Conservative MP Leslyn Lewis, who wondered aloud on social media whether a basic income will end up becoming Trudeau's policy Hail Mary, with his party continuing to tank in the polls.
On Dec. 29 in sharing Conservative Leader Pierre Poilievre's new video on "debtonation" on Facebook, Lewis blamed the federal Liberals for creating "the debt problem that's connected to our inflation crisis" and wondered if Canadians would trust them again once "our traditional safety nets are destroyed."
"My guess is after they burn it all down, they will propose a solution where they have control: let's see ... a digital and programmable Universal Basic Income perhaps?" she asked.
Many progressive senators and Liberal MPs have been championing the idea of a basic income for years, and it seemed to take on new life during the pandemic as Ottawa rolled out large, widespread income supports.
But even then, Trudeau did not seem very enthusiastic about the idea: When he was asked about it, he said he was more interested in staying focused on controlling COVID-19.
The notion of a basic income is not new. Nobel laureate and free-market economist Milton Friedman championed the idea of a negative income tax - which would provide a certain basic income to people below a certain earning threshold, while eliminating all other government welfare programs.
Friedman's version, promising more efficiency, less bureaucracy and the freedom for recipients to spend their income as they see fit, appeals to some conservatives.
When he was the opposition finance critic, Poilievre wrote in the National Post in 2018 that "many basic income supporters have the right goal." Deputy party leader Melissa Lantsman told broadcaster Stephen LeDrew in 2020 that she "somewhat agree(s) with that" and that "Conservatives can own" the issue of a basic income.
The progressive version is different: Politicians and economists on the left have been instead championing a basic income on top of other welfare subsidies. They argue that the income would in time lift recipients out of poverty, reducing their reliance on other social supports.
"We're talking about insuring that people have adequate incomes but we're not talking about some Keynesian model that leaves everybody with just a bit of money and no other supports," said Sen. Pate last year.
That's why UBC's Milligan compares trying to define a basic income to nailing Jell-O to a wall. "Because any time you kind of nail it down to what you think they mean, it changes," he said.
Senator Pate, having been appointed under Trudeau, comes at it from the progressive side. She said Canada already provides supports similar to a basic income for some groups: the Guaranteed Income Supplement for low-income seniors; the Canada Child Benefit for families with children; and the soon-to-be Canada Disability Benefit for people with disabilities, for example.
"It's like we're inching toward it. Why not just take the next step?" she said.
But passing bills that are not government priorities can be a tedious and long process. S-233 was first introduced in the Senate in December 2021, at the same time as an identical one, C-223, was introduced in the House of Commons by NDP MP Leah Gazan.
Gazan's bill has remained untouched in the House, whereas the Senate bill was adopted on division in second reading in April 2023 to be further studied at committee. Even Conservative senators agreed to take a further look at S-233, despite claiming their caucus does not support it.
"I think that the light of day will expose the weaknesses in this bill," said the Conservative party's critic for the bill, Nova Scotia Senator Michael L. MacDonald, at the time. Sen. Pate, however, claims that there are "many, many people within all of the parties who are supportive" of her bill, and she remains hopeful its study in committee will wrap up soon after the Senate returns next month and be submitted to a final vote in the Upper Chamber in the spring.
Attempts have been made in the past to pilot basic income projects, but any promising results ran up against serious cost concerns. There was Manitoba's "Mincome" in the 1970s, Ontario's brief pilot in 2017-2018, and P.E.I. has been running a targeted basic income pilot program with a few hundred recipients since 2021.
British Columbia and Quebec have studied it but concluded that a targeted approach is better than a universal basic income.
"The needs of people in this society are too diverse to be effectively answered simply with a cheque from the government," wrote the B.C. Basic Income panel in its December 2020 report on the concept. "A basic income is a very costly approach to addressing any specific goal, such as poverty reduction."
The Parliamentary Budget Officer produced an analysis in 2021 that concluded that a national guaranteed basic income using parameters of Ontario's 2017 pilot project could cut Canada's poverty rate by half in just one year, but at a hefty cost of $91 billion in 2024-25 and $93 billion in 2025-26. That's nearly double what Ottawa spends, for example, on health transfers to the provinces.
"So I think the probability of a UBI (universal basic income) being implemented in Canada on a widespread basis, not as small pilot or something, is very close to zero," said Milligan.
Even Sen. Pate admits that there are impediments for political parties to move forward with a basic income, given the initial cost of the measure and the fact that any given party is unlikely to reap all of the benefits of cracking down on poverty in Canada in one electoral cycle. But she remains hopeful that she will see some sort of basic income become reality in her lifetime.
"I think it's much like medicare," she said. "Many people thought it was a good idea, (but) people were afraid what it would cost and afraid that they wouldn't see the benefit within the electoral cycle. And then eventually, Saskatchewan just decided to do it and once it started rolling out, everybody wanted in."
Milligan said he agrees with the B.C. panel's approach, which is to "roll up your sleeves and do the hard work of expanding, improving, replacing, revamping our existing social support system."
"It's complicated, it's messy, it's hard work," he said. "I think a lot of the basic income stuff is just people looking for a magical solution to a tough problem."
National Post calevesque@postmedia.com

Document 99 of 108

Falling into the 'population trap'

Author: Kheiriddin, Tasha

Publication info: Kheiriddin, Tasha. 

https://libproxy.wlu.ca/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/falling-into-population-trap/docview/2916558941/se-2?accountid=15090

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://ocul-wlu.primo.exlibrisgroup.com/discovery/openurl?institution=01OCUL_WLU&amp;vid=01OCUL_WLU:WLU_DEF&amp;?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=unknown&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Acanadiannews&amp;atitle=Falling+into+the+%27population+trap%27&amp;title=The+Windsor+Star&amp;issn=&amp;date=2024-01-19&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.5&amp;au=Kheiriddin%2C+Tasha&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=The+Windsor+Star&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: Canada's immigration policies are damaging the economy. That's the finding of Canada's top bank economists, who issued a dire warning to the federal government. By admitting 455,000 new permanent residents and more than 800,000 non-permanent residents last year, Canada is on the brink of a recession, or worse.
"I'll put it bluntly: We've fallen into the population trap," said Stéfane Marion, chief economist at National Bank of Canada. An increase in the standard of living is no longer possible because "you don't have enough savings to stabilize your capital-to-labour ratio."
We also learned that the government was warned two years ago by its own officials about high immigration causing a housing shortage - and ignored those warnings.
When asked about this, Housing Minister Sean Fraser replied, "I don't think anybody needs a briefing note to understand that having more people in the housing market impacts the housing market, but having gone through in consecutive years the process of building Canada's immigration levels plan, I can tell you that there are an enormous number of factors that go into them."
OK then, so what are those factors? Why did the government decide that immigration levels should increase at this pace? Why does it continue to allow hundreds of thousands of foreign students to work unlimited hours? Why did it admit so many people into the country in such a short amount of time? The classic answer is that we need to increase the labour supply. Businesses are short of workers. Canadians are not having enough children to replace them. That's the argument of the Century Initiative, the business community and immigration advocates.
But simply pulling in adult immigrants and temporary workers is not the same as boosting fertility in the existing population. Those workers have needs that are very different from those of children.
They won't live under their parents'roof; they need their own housing. They won't be ferried about by their parents; they need independent transportation. They will be buying adult levels of food, clothing and other necessities. In short, they will be competing with other adults for basic needs, and that increased demand means two things: price inflation and resource scarcity. Which is exactly what Canada is now experiencing.
Fraser is right. You don't need a study to figure this out. It's simple common sense. So again, why did the government do this - especially after it was warned not to by its own bureaucrats? The cynic in me sees one reason: votes.
Immigrants tend to settle in cities. City dwellers tend to vote for "progressive" parties. In Canada, since the 1960s that has translated to increased Liberal support. According to research done at Western University, a oneunit shift in the "urbanity score" for a riding produces an increase of eight percentage points in the Liberal party's vote share in that riding. No surprise then, that in the 2021 election, the Liberals won 109 of the 150 most urban ridings, while the Conservatives took only 23.
Immigrants are also grateful to the party that brings them in. Prime Minister Justin Trudeau learned this from his father. Pierre Elliott Trudeau took credit for the citizenship of millions of Canadians, even though many had come to Canada under previous governments. Why? Because they took their oath under his watch. And when it came to elections, the immigrant communities of the day - Italians, Greeks, Haitians, Vietnamese - tended to vote Liberal.
The implications of Trudeau Jr.'s replication of this policy are dire. Studies show that they are turning Canadians against immigration: even 62 per cent of current immigrants think we're letting in too many people. They are impoverishing Canadians, both current and newcomer, according to the bank study. And they are also helping fuel Quebec separatism, as francophones look with alarm at rising immigrant populations in the Rest of Canada. By the end of the century, Quebec risks becoming a bit player in Confederation, with only 15 per cent of its population.
This cannot continue. Instead of flooding the country with newcomers, the government needs to boost domestic productivity. That's a harder fix - and one that won't give them more votes. But then again, if Canadians can't afford a decent life, the Liberals may not get their votes, either. Tasha Kheiriddin is Postmedia's national politics columnist.

Consultant now heads anti-Israel TV channel; Feds cancelled contract after vitriolic posts

Author: Dawson, Tyler

Publication info: Dawson, Tyler. 

https://libproxy.wlu.ca/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/consultant-now-heads-anti-israel-tv-channel-feds/docview/2916554818/se-2?accountid=15090

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://ocul-wlu.primo.exlibrisgroup.com/discovery/openurl?institution=01OCUL_WLU&amp;vid=01OCUL_WLU:WLU_DEF&amp;?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Acanadiannews&amp;atitle=Consultant+now+heads+anti-Israel+TV+channel%3B+Feds+cancelled+contract+after+vitriolic+posts&amp;title=The+Windsor+Star&amp;issn=&amp;date=2024-01-19&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=NP.2&amp;au=Dawson%2C+Tyler&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=The+Windsor+Star&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: Laith Marouf, the government-funded anti-racism consultant whose record of hateful tweets became an embarrassing controversy for the Liberals, is now the director of an anti-Israel online broadcaster.
Free Palestine Television (FPTV) was launched after the Hamas massacre of Israeli civilians on Oct. 7 and has featured footage of Hamas's attacks on Israeli soldiers as well as manoeuvres from Hamas-affiliated militias such as the Mujahideen Brigades and Al-Qassam Brigades, which operate out of Gaza and the West Bank. It also published speeches with live translation to English from Hezbollah leader Sayyed Hassan Nasrallah and it publishes press releases attributed to various terror groups, such as Islamic Resistance in Iraq, an umbrella group of Iran-backed militias.
Marouf, who's identified on its site as FPTV's founder, is a senior consultant with the Community Media Advocacy Centre, which had a $122,000 contract with the Liberal government in 2022 to develop an anti-racism strategy for the broadcasting sector.
It subsequently emerged that Marouf had a history of hateful social media posts about Jews, francophones and Black people.
One read: "You know all those loud mouthed bags of human feces, a.k.a. the Jewish White Supremacists; when we liberate Palestine and they have to go back to where they come from, they will return to being low voiced b-----s of their Christian/Secular White Supremacist Masters."
The tweets were exposed in news reports and the government cancelled the contract in August 2022, although it was revealed that the federal government had known about the tweets a month earlier and had done nothing.
Marouf 's lawyer at the time said the posts were not directed at all Jews, just certain ones.
Canadian Heritage has since changed the way it vets funding requests for community and anti-racism projects.
T he federal government has sought to recoupits funds from Marouf through a collections agency, the Canada Revenue Agency and the courts, but has reported no success. Reports in October 2022 said Marouf was living in Beirut.
Since the scandal over funding to Marouf's organization, he has become a regular on social media programs, offering opinions on Palestinian politics, Israel and other geopolitical issues. He has also appeared on Critical Hour, a podcast hosted by Sputnik News, a state-owned Russian broadcaster, and on programs by Russian propaganda network Russia Today.
FPTV's website says it launched "with the outbreak of the Al-Aqsa Flood" - the name Hamas and other Palestinian groups have given to the Oct. 7 massacre. The online station is based out of Lebanon, and is staffed primarily by students, professors and community members, Marouf said in an interview.
"Knowing that there is a huge gap left in news programming in English that is supportive of resistance and the right to liberation ... we decided to bring together our communities here to start a project, a community television station," Marouf said in the interview with Activist News Network in November 2023.
The Middle East Forum, an American conser vative think tank, published a report on Friday claiming that FPTV had links to the Lebanon-based group Al-Tajammu. According to Michael Barak, a researcher at the Institute for Counter-Terrorism at Israel's Reichmann University, Al-Tajammu is "an international pro-Iranian platform to leverage the resistance axis against the U.S., Israel and their allies."
Marouf described the attacks on Al-Tajammu as "frivolous" and accused the Middle East Forum of being a "racist organization."
Marouf and FPTV also downplayed any connections to Al-Tajammu.
In a series of posts to X on Saturday, FPTV alleged that "the largest Jewish White Supremacist accounts in Apartheid Canada are mounting a disinformation campaign against our Community Television station," and that it "is unaffiliated with any political party or groups, including Al-Tajammu which hosted our studios for a brief time & we have since moved as we expanded."
The Middle East Forum report, and subsequent social media posts, suggested that Free Palestine TV was "established" in Canada.
Marouf told National Post: "It's not established in Canada. It's not based in Canada. There's no connection there."
In mid-December, FPTV hosted a fundraiser for the channel on Zoom, which featured guest speakers who are prominent figures in Indigenous advocacy in Canada. Among them were Sleydo', also known as Molly Wickham, a Wet'suwet'en chief and prominent opponent of the Coastal GasLink pipeline in British Columbia; Judy Da Silva, a prominent member of the Grassy Narrows First Nation in northwestern Ontario; and Isaac Murdoch, an artist and member of the Serpent River First Nation. National Post did not attend the event and was unable to confirm that all the guests appeared.
National Post

Service Ontario closures ahead: report; In brief

Links: https://ocul-wlu.primo.exlibrisgroup.com/discovery/openurl?institution=01OCUL_WLU&amp;vid=01OCUL_WLU:WLU_DEF&amp;?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Acanadiannews&amp;atitle=Service+Ontario+closures+ahead%3A+report%3B+In+brief&amp;title=The+Windsor+Star&amp;issn=&amp;date=2024-01-13&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.6&amp;au=&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=The+Windsor+Star&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: The Doug Ford government is reportedly shuttering an unspecified number of ServiceOntario locations. CityNews reported Wednesday that the Ontario outlets where people can renew driver's licences and health cards will be replaced by kiosks inside select Staples Canada stores. A Dec. 7 news release announced the ServiceOntario-Staples Canada partnership, a pilot project that would see ServiceOntario kiosks open in Staples stores to "reduce the overall cost to deliver government services to the public." That news release didn't mention any pending ServiceOntario closures, however. It was not clear how many ServiceOntario locations would be affected.

Affected employees would be given opportunities to work at the new Staples Canada kiosks, according to a government spokesperson, the report said. ServiceOntario completes 59 million transactions annually and more than 24 million transactions are completed in person at 275 ServiceOntario centres.

Document 102 of 108

Federal parties haunted by elections past

Author: Kheiriddin, Tasha

Publication info: Kheiriddin, Tasha. 

https://libproxy.wlu.ca/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/federal-parties-haunted-elections-past/docview/2913608302/se-2?accountid=15090

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://ocul-wlu.primo.exlibrisgroup.com/discovery/openurl?institution=01OCUL_WLU&amp;vid=01OCUL_WLU:WLU_DEF&amp;?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=unknown&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Acanadiannews&amp;atitle=Federal+parties+haunted+by+elections+past&amp;title=The+Windsor+Star&amp;issn=&amp;date=2024-01-12&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.6&amp;au=Kheiriddin%2C+Tasha&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=The+Windsor+Star&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: Settle in, voters of Canada: the next federal election likely won't be until 2025. That's an eon away in political terms, and anything - literally anything - can happen in that time. If you had said in January 2023 that one year later, the Middle East would be a war zone, the federal government would call a foreign interference inquiry and the Conservatives would be trouncing the Liberals at the polls, most people would have called you crazy - or at least hoped you were wrong about the first one.
That said, there are a few certainties all three major federal parties will face in the lead-up to the next vote. And we got a taste of them in the past two weeks, a reminder that ghosts of elections past are always hanging around, ready to haunt you in elections future if you're not careful.
Let's start with the Liberals. Their Achilles heel is elitism, making them easy to brand as out of touch with the average Canadian. That elitism was on full display over Christmas when Prime Minister Justin Trudeau jetted off to Jamaica for a family vacation. And not just any vacation: this wasn't a Beaches or Sandals-level trip, but a $9,300-a-night epic getaway at a posh resort, gifted by owner and family friend Peter Green. Total tab: $84,000.
To put that into perspective, that same $84,000, if you had it lying around, would qualify you to buy a $660,000 Toronto condo at current mortgage rates - if your household income was $150,000 or more. The median Toronto household earns just over half that, according to the last census. In other words, for the price of Trudeau's holiday, they can't even afford to buy a home - a fact the Conservatives will likely point out at every opportunity.
But the Tories need to be careful, too.
Their Achilles heel is Uncle Marvin. As in your crazy Uncle Marvin, who trots out his collection of tin foil hats every time the family gets together for Thanksgiving. Uncle Marvin says a lot of nutty things, and a lot of not-so-nice things, too. He rants about gay people burning in lakes of fire and Jews running the world. He has surfaced in various political campaigns throughout the ages, and it has never ended well.
Uncle Marvin came out of the woodwork this week, in the form of a petition sponsored by Conservative MP Leslyn Lewis. The petition asks for Canada to quit the United Nations, and is the brainchild of a man named Doug Porter, who told the left-leaning website Press Progress that Lewis's staff helped him with the wording. Porter also said that if the UN gets what it wants, "It would be the elimination of a majority of the population and those that would remain would be slaves, and they would be repeatedly injected with mRNA injections to change their DNA so they lose their humanity and they are different creatures." Liberal attack ads, start your engines.
As for the NDP, their biggest issue is trust.
By 2025, they will have been propping up this government for a full four years. So when Leader Jagmeet Singh recently said, "that's off the table" next time around, it's kind of hard to believe him.
Of course, Singh's pension will have vested by then, so perhaps there is less risk in having the government fall, especially when his own party only gave him an 81 per cent approval rating at its last national convention.
Cynical? Yes. But this is politics, and cynicism is par for the course. The NDP will also need to keep its radical wing in check if it is to stop the labour vote from migrating to the Conservatives. This is where issues like the Hamas-Israel war come into play - if union voters feel the NDP has become too "progressive" for their tastes on issues like Palestinian statehood, they may tune out, and tune into the Tories'strong economic message instead.
Ultimately, the next election will be decided in the pocketbook. If the economy turns around, the Liberals could see a boost - but they will still need other issues to stay in power. So watch for those ghosts to keep haunting us throughout 2024 - and into the next new year. Tasha Kheiriddin is Postmedia's national politics columnist.

Dilkens floats 3.93% tax increase in 2024 Windsor budget

Author: Campbell, Taylor

Publication info: Campbell, Taylor. 

https://libproxy.wlu.ca/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/blogs-podcasts-websites/dilkens-floats-3-93-tax-increase-2024-windsor/docview/2912054840/se-2?accountid=15090

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://ocul-wlu.primo.exlibrisgroup.com/discovery/openurl?institution=01OCUL_WLU&amp;vid=01OCUL_WLU:WLU_DEF&amp;?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Acanadiannews&amp;atitle=Dilkens+floats+3.93%25+tax+increase+in+2024+Windsor+budget&amp;title=The+Windsor+Star+%28Online%29&amp;issn=&amp;date=2024-01-08&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=&amp;au=Campbell%2C+Taylor&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=The+Windsor+Star+%28Online%29&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: Windsor Mayor Drew Dilkens has tabled the city's 2024 budget with a proposed 3.93 per cent property tax increase.
Touting another year of "holding the line" on taxes - by keeping the hike below inflation, despite the more conventional definition of maintaining the same position - Dilkens on Monday said the budget focuses on keeping existing services with "some minor enhancements where possible."
"What you'll find here are very sensible, almost pain-free proposals to continue service delivery in 2024 much the same as you've seen in 2023," he told reporters during a budget presentation at city hall.
To keep the proposed tax increase as low as possible, Dilkens said the city searched for efficiencies and "tweaks" where it can to create savings. For example, the proposed budget recommends a $500,000 reduction to Windsor's winter maintenance budget, which has a $1-million year-end surplus.
Of the proposed 3.93-per-cent tax increase, only 0.18 per cent will go toward discretionary spending, and the rest will cover contractual obligation and inflationary pressures, Dilkens said.
The budget package includes a proposed $1.9-billion, 10-year capital plan with more than $200 million in capital investments for 2024 - a 24-per-cent increase in annual capital spending from eight years ago, the city said.
Among those capital projects are more than $25 million for sewer master plan and flood mitigation program implementation, more than $22 million for road rehabilitation, nearly $8.6 million for the upcoming phase of the Adie Knox Herman Recreation Complex reimagining project, and roughly $5.7 million for improvements to the Transit Windsor garage.
This year, the city also plans to spend $4.37 million on infrastructure improvements at Lauzon Parkway and County Road 42.
Over the next 10 years, the city would spend nearly $569 million on roads, nearly $471 million on sewers, and more than $183 million on parks and recreation, if council approves.
A copy of the proposed 2024 City of Windsor Budget is shown on Monday, Jan. 8, 2023.
"This 2024 budget is a delicate balance of compromises and investments and ensures the continued delivery of municipal service and access to amenities," Dilkens said. "It also recognizes the need for future growth planning without jeopardizing our financial stability.
"Through this budget, we are being responsible and fiscally prudent and continuing to be forward thinking."
Also outlined in the 10-year capital budget are $179.1 million for transportation, $168.6 million for corporate property infrastructure, $143.3 million for city agencies, boards, and committees, $115.3 million for community and economic development, $44.9 million for corporate technology, and $19.9 million for capital reserve replenishment.
Under changes to the Municipal Act, heads of council with strong mayor powers, Dilkens included, are now required to table the annual municipal budget. Although council has the power to make amendments to the proposed budget, strong mayors can veto those changes. Councillors can only override the veto with a two-thirds majority vote.
Dilkens said he "can't answer" whether he'll further use the powers afforded to him by the Doug Ford Progressive Conservatives during budget deliberations.
"We'll just see how sensible the amendments are, and we'll go through the process together. That's the commitment I made to all the members of city council, that we're gonna figure this out together."
The proposed budget is available online at the city's website, citywindsor.ca. Members of the public who want to share their thoughts on the budget with council can sign up as a delegate to speak at a special council meeting on Jan. 22.
Council is scheduled to decide on the budget Feb. 6.
Last year, council approved a property tax increase of 4.48 per cent.
Since 2006, Windsor residents have seen a total tax levy increase of 12 per cent, while the provincial average for that time period is 67 per cent, the city said.
The average Windsor property owner pays $849 less each on water, waste water, and property taxes than the typical Ontario property owner.
tcampbell@postmedia.com
twitter.com/wstarcampbell
Windsor Mayor Drew Dilkens presents the proposed 2024 city budget during a news conference at city hall on Monday, Jan. 8, 2024.

'Hired to be fired' - City engineer and solicitor latest of several departures from top city positions

Author: Campbell, Taylor

Publication info: Campbell, Taylor. 

https://libproxy.wlu.ca/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/blogs-podcasts-websites/hired-be-fired-city-engineer-solicitor-latest/docview/2910940277/se-2?accountid=15090

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://ocul-wlu.primo.exlibrisgroup.com/discovery/openurl?institution=01OCUL_WLU&amp;vid=01OCUL_WLU:WLU_DEF&amp;?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Acanadiannews&amp;atitle=%27Hired+to+be+fired%27+-+City+engineer+and+solicitor+latest+of+several+departures+from+top+city+positions&amp;title=The+Windsor+Star+%28Online%29&amp;issn=&amp;date=2024-01-06&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=&amp;au=Campbell%2C+Taylor&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=The+Windsor+Star+%28Online%29&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: Terminations, sudden retirements, and unexplained departures.
In recent years, a number of top bureaucrats at Windsor city hall and other city-funded organizations have been plucked from their gruelling pedestals in the name of "organizational restructuring," while others have purportedly leapt off by choice.
With Windsor Mayor Drew Dilkens flexing his strong mayor powers to unilaterally turf members of the city's corporate leadership team - something he told the Star is now his "responsibility" to do, not the council majority's - experts are weighing in.
Senior municipal management is a "very tough job" that will grow more difficult while the boundaries of strong mayor powers are sorted out, said Zachary Spicer, a York University associate professor in the School of Public Policy and Administration.
"We'd still love to see where the boundaries of these powers are because, frankly, the legislation was drafted and passed so quickly that we didn't have a lot of time to put scrutiny on it," he said.
"There's going to be growing pains," said Spicer, who focuses on municipal affairs. He spoke with the Star following the dismissal of city engineer Chris Nepszy and solicitor Shelby Askin Hager in the fall.
To better understand what's going on in Ontario municipalities with strong mayors at the helm, Spicer cited Doug Ford's 2016 book, Ford Nation: Two Brothers, One Vision. Written before Ford entered provincial politics, the book, Spicer said, has a section about how Ford would introduce strong mayor powers if he ever became premier.
"The whole idea there was really about accountability. He said there has to be someone to blame when things go wrong," Spicer said.
"If the buck stops with the mayor, the mayor has to have certain pathways to get things done. But the mayor also has to be responsible for them.
"I think the mayor of Windsor has embodied a little bit of that."
Nepszy and Askin Hager are among at least eight prominent local officials, including four top city hall bureaucrats, who have vacated city-funded positions in the last two-and-a-half years. Nepszy and Askin Hager were the first two axed using strong mayor powers.
"City managers are kind of like hockey coaches. They're hired to be fired," Spicer said. "These people don't have huge, long tenures.
"It's a very tough job, especially when you have to stick-handle everything through a council that is sometimes divided, sometimes giving you very different, very mixed messages."
Windsor City Hall is shown on Wednesday, January 3, 2024.
In an interview with the Star, Dilkens said the "tone gets set from the top" at the city, and top senior leadership jobs are "tough positions."
"They are grinding positions - it's not just an 8:30 to 4:30 type operation. It's a whole lot more. They work very, very hard, and they're very dedicated people.
"We need to make sure we have the right people at the top to set the tone. That definitely trickles down to the rest of the 3,000 employees who work for the city."
The relatively recent exodus from senior city-funded positions began in June of 2021 with the termination of city clerk Valerie Critchley. Her dismissal happened amid a big departmental reshuffling under then-chief administrative officer Jason Reynar.
Critchley's role as a corporate team leader was effectively eliminated, and her responsibilities were added to other divisions. The move spearheaded by Reynar was approved during an in-camera meeting of city council.
The role of city clerk was retained due to a legal requirement set out in the Municipal Act, but the job fell under a different division and is now occupied by Steve Vlachodimos. Critchley had been city clerk for about 14 years and before that served as a city lawyer and manager of risk management.
Windsor Mayor Drew Dilkens speaks during a regular meeting of city council on Monday, June 12, 2023.
Next, then-Windsor police chief Pam Mizuno suddenly announced her retirement in March 2022, halfway through her first five-year contract and following the Ambassador Bridge blockade, saying she was bowing out to spend more time with her two daughters. She was named acting chief in July 2019, when predecessor Al Frederick announced his retirement.
The police chief's job is governed by the Windsor Police Services Board, which Dilkens chairs.
Mizuno was Windsor's first officer appointed to the provincial proceeds-of-crime unit, the city's first female deputy chief and its first female chief. She was replaced by Jason Bellaire, who served as deputy chief under Mizuno and interim chief following her retirement.
Next on the chopping block at city hall was CAO Reynar himself. One week after Mizuno announced her retirement and just a year after council brought him in to fill the top job at city hall, council in April 2022 announced Reynar's immediate departure.
An email from Mayor Dilkens to municipal staff at the time said council had opted "to move in a different direction." The decision to do so "was very difficult ... but one we felt was right to help meet the future needs of our community," the email said.
A lot of us were very shocked by that
A lot of folks in the Toronto area who pay attention to municipal politics "took notice" and found it "very surprising" when Reynar was terminated, York professor Spicer said.
"Jason Reynar has such a good reputation. Given that he's coming from the (Greater Toronto Area), I think a lot of us in the GTA saw him as a real up-and-comer," Spicer said. "Getting the top job at a big city like Windsor was not surprising, but given his tenure didn't last long, I think a lot of us were very shocked by that."
Reynar had taken over from veteran CAO Onorio Colucci, who worked at city hall for 37 years before retiring. Colucci returned on an interim basis following Reynar's dismissal, until a new CAO - city chief financial officer and treasurer Joe Mancina - was named in April 2023.
Also in April, the mayor's chief of staff, Andrew Teliszewsky, vacated his position for a job in the private sector. Teliszewsky filled the shoes of former chief of staff Norma Coleman in 2020 following her retirement.
Teliszewsky's temporary replacement, Abe Taqtaq, still holds the job nearly nine months later, though Dilkens told the Star he's still looking for a permanent hire. Taqtaq was the election campaign manager for Dilkens and for former mayor Eddie Francis.
Windsor Mayor Drew Dilkens, left, speaks with Andrew Teliszewsky, 38, newly appointed Chief of Staff at City Council Meeting Monday March 2, 2020.
In May 2023, Windsor Public Library CEO Kitty Pope's unexpected retirement was confirmed in an internal memo sent to library staff. Chris Woodrow, the library's director of corporate services, filled in as acting CEO following a library board meeting held at city hall in April that included an in-camera item, the nature of which was not specified.
Pope had served as the library's CEO since 2014. Her retirement came as the library board prepared to hunt for a new central library branch location - a replacement for the one at 850 Ouellette Ave. vacated under Pope's tenure. No update on the central library has been announced in the months since, and a temporary downtown branch continues to operate in the Paul Martin Building.
Dana Paladino, the city's deputy solicitor, served as acting library CEO until mid-November, when Michael Chantler, the city's manager of aquatics, was named the new acting library CEO.
The Windsor Public Library is governed by its board, not by city council. However, three councillors - Ward 4's Mark McKenzie, Ward 3's Renaldo Agostino, and Ward 9's Kieran McKenzie - sit on the board with three citizen members.
In July, the Windsor-Detroit Tunnel Corporation, which is chaired by Dilkens, announced the appointment of a new chief executive officer to replace Carolyn Brown. Although the announcement made no mention of why Brown was leaving the position she held for more than eight years, Dilkens told the Star her contract had ended.
Brown was replaced by Tal Czudner, a long-time friend of Mayor Dilkens, on Aug. 1, following a hiring process undertaken by an independent third party. Czudner's selection was approved by a committee of the tunnel board.
Carolyn Brown, CEO of the Windsor Detroit Tunnel Corporation is shown at her office in Windsor on Monday, March 6, 2023.
In November, Dilkens exerted his strong mayor powers to dismiss city engineer Nepszy. Retired city engineer Mark Winterton has returned to fill the role temporarily.
In a separate order at the same time, Dilkens dismissed city solicitor Askin Hager and removed the solicitor position from the corporate leadership team structure. Under a new and reorganized leadership structure, the city solicitor reports to the commissioner of corporate services.
For Spicer, the number of changes to senior city-funded positions in Windsor doesn't necessarily matter. He said he's seeing a lot of movement happening in other municipalities, with senior leaders taking jobs in other cities, and seeking more flexible and secure work arrangements in the private sector.
In places where a mayor "may be difficult to work with or have a vision that's very different" from someone in a senior leadership position, that individual may pre-emptively move on.
Municipalities have also seen a rash of retirements, Spicer said. While staff at the provincial and federal level tend to "come in as interns and stay for 25 years," that's not usually the case in the municipal sector, which "traditionally has had problems recruiting and retaining folks."
Dilkens told the Star he's "not concerned at all" about turnover at the city, and that turnover is "relatively low."
But the COVID-19 pandemic was a "very trying time for people, especially at the municipal level," Dilkens said. "It burned a lot of people out," people who re-evaluated their lives and decided to retire or change careers.
"Ultimately, my goal is, whether it was pre-COVID, post-COVID, or ... for the mayor who's here 25 or 50 or 100 years from now, is to make sure you have the right people in the right places at the right time to deliver for the residents."

Hired to be fired'; Windsor's top engineer, solicitor latest high-level bureaucrats at city-funded organizations to be let go

Author: Campbell, Taylor

Publication info: Campbell, Taylor. 

https://libproxy.wlu.ca/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/hired-be-fired-windsors-top-engineer-solicitor/docview/2910903041/se-2?accountid=15090

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://ocul-wlu.primo.exlibrisgroup.com/discovery/openurl?institution=01OCUL_WLU&amp;vid=01OCUL_WLU:WLU_DEF&amp;?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=article&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Acanadiannews&amp;atitle=Hired+to+be+fired%27%3B+Windsor%27s+top+engineer%2C+solicitor+latest+high-level+bureaucrats+at+city-funded+organizations+to+be+let+go&amp;title=The+Windsor+Star&amp;issn=&amp;date=2024-01-06&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.3&amp;au=Campbell%2C+Taylor&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=The+Windsor+Star&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: Terminations, sudden retirements, and unexplained departures.
In recent years, a number of top bureaucrats at Windsor city hall and other city-funded organizations have been plucked from their gruelling pedestals in the name of "organizational restructuring," while others have purportedly leapt off by choice.
With Windsor Mayor Drew Dilkens flexing his strong mayor powers to unilaterally turf members of the city's corporate leadership team - something he told the Star is now his "responsibility" to do, not the council majority's - experts are weighing in.
Senior municipal management is a "very tough job" that will grow more difficult while the boundaries of strong mayor powers are sorted out, said Zachary Spicer, a York University associate professor in the School of Public Policy and Administration.
"We'd still love to see where the boundaries of these powers are because, frankly, the legislation was drafted and passed so quickly that we didn't have a lot of time to put scrutiny on it," he said.
"There's going to be growing pains," said Spicer, who focuses on municipal affairs.
He spoke with the Star following the dismissal of city engineer Chris Nepszy and solicitor Shelby Askin Hager in the fall.
To better understand what's going on in Ontario municipalities with strong mayors at the helm, Spicer cited Doug Ford's 2016 book, Ford Nation: Two Brothers, One Vision. Written before Ford entered provincial politics, the book, Spicer said, has a section about how Ford would introduce strong mayor powers if he ever became premier.
"The whole idea there was really about accountability. He said there has to be someone to blame when things go wrong," Spicer said.
"If the buck stops with the mayor, the mayor has to have certain pathways to get things done. But the mayor also has to be responsible for them.
"I think the mayor of Windsor has embodied a little bit of that."
Nepszy and Askin Hager are among at least eight prominent local officials, including four top city hall bureaucrats, who have vacated city-funded positions in the last two-and-a-half years. Nepszy and Askin Hager were the first two axed using strong mayor powers.
"City managers are kind of like hockey coaches. They're hired to be fired," Spicer said. "These people don't have huge, long tenures.
"It's a very tough job, especially when you have to stickhandle everything through a council that is sometimes divided, sometimes giving you very different, very mixed messages."
In an interview with the Star, Dilkens said the "tone gets set from the top" at the city, and top senior leadership jobs are "tough positions."
"They are grinding positions - it's not just an 8:30 to 4:30 type operation.
It's a whole lot more. They work very, very hard, and they're very dedicated people.
"We need to make sure we have the right people at the top to set the tone. That definitely trickles down to the rest of the 3,000 employees who work for the city."
The relatively recent exodus from senior city-funded positions began in June of 2021 with the termination of city clerk Valerie Critchley. Her dismissal happened amid a big departmental reshuffling under then-chief administrative officer Jason Reynar.
Critchley's role as a corporate team leader was effectively eliminated, and her responsibilities were added to other divisions. The move spearheaded by Reynar was approved during an in-camera meeting of city council.
The role of city clerk was retained due to a legal requirement set out in the Municipal Act, but the job fell under a different division and is now occupied by Steve Vlachodimos. Critchley had been city clerk for about 14 years and before that served as a city lawyer and manager of risk management.

She was named acting chief in July 2019, when predecessor Al Frederick announced his retirement.
The police chief's job is governed by the Windsor Police Services Board, which Dilkens chairs.
Mizuno was Windsor's first officer appointed to the provincial proceeds-of-crime unit, the city's first female deputy chief and its first female chief. She was replaced by Jason Bellaire, who served as deputy chief under Mizuno and interim chief following her retirement.

Document 106 of 108

Ontario's new Liberal leader faces perilous political journey

Author: Brown-John, Lloyd

Publication info: Brown-John, Lloyd. 

https://libproxy.wlu.ca/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/ontarios-new-liberal-leader-faces-perilous/docview/2910903007/se-2?accountid=15090

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://ocul-wlu.primo.exlibrisgroup.com/discovery/openurl?institution=01OCUL_WLU&amp;vid=01OCUL_WLU:WLU_DEF&amp;?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=unknown&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Acanadiannews&amp;atitle=Ontario%27s+new+Liberal+leader+faces+perilous+political+journey&amp;title=The+Windsor+Star&amp;issn=&amp;date=2024-01-06&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.8&amp;au=Brown-John%2C+Lloyd&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=The+Windsor+Star&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: In the torrential rush to prepare for Thanksgiving, Christmas, Black Friday, Cyber Monday, New Year's Eve, National Cookie Day, or Maple Syrup Day or Eat a Red Apple Day, you may have missed the news that Ontario's provincial Liberal party has a new leader.
Bonnie Crombie won leadership of Ontario's rather obscure Liberal party. She outpolled three other contenders. Her success on Dec. 2 incidentally coincided with World Pear Day.
Crombie has been mayor of Mississauga, that rambling suburb of Toronto, since December 2014. As of Jan. 14, she will be taking on, and seeking to cope with, Ontario's one step forward and two steps backward dancer, Premier Doug Ford.
Crombie indicated in November on TVO that she would run for Ontario's legislature in 2026 - the next scheduled provincial election.
Crombie is an experienced political animal. A youthful 63, she earned a BA and an MBA from the University of Toronto. Her birth parents were of Polish origin. From 2008 to 2011, she sat as a Liberal member of Parliament. She was defeated by a Conservative in the 2011 federal election. That same year, she was elected as a City of Mississauga municipal councillor. In 2014, long-serving Mississauga mayor Hazel McCallion retired and she gave her support to Crombie, who won a close victory for the mayoralty job over a fellow city council member. She was re-elected with substantial majorities in 2018 and 2022.
Mississauga is a rather astonishing city, with a population that rose from 172,000 (1971) to more than 800,000 (2022), Some 60 Fortune 500 companies base their global or Canadian headquarters in Mississauga - names like Walmart, FedEx and UPS.
With the City of Brampton and Town of Caledon, the city is part of the upper-tier Regional Municipality of Peel. Mississauga is where the big money resides, hence its desire to split from its municipal partners.
Throughout her mayoral tenure, Crombie advocated for dissolution of the upper-tier regional municipality. In 2023, Ford's Progressive Conservative government supported that split with the Hazel McCallion Bill (a Bill to Dissolve Peel Region) tabled in the Ontario Legislature last May. However, Ford's minister of municipal affairs, Paul Calandra, announced a rollback of the prospective full dissolution on Dec. 13.
Crombie was not happy and said so in a news conference after Calandra and Ford's retreat from what she referred to as "the right thing," or what Hazel McCallion had wanted.
Reasonably, you might ask, why should Crombie's battle with Ford's government over Peel matter to those of us dwelling in Ontario's Deep South? Crombie, now leader of a rump Liberal party in the legislature, continues to focus and fight a battle largely of interest to only those on the western fringe of the Greater Toronto Area.
Ford has yet to demonstrate that he has a working perspective of an entire province as opposed to an introspective view of the world from the GTA. Consider the mess and scandal of the Greenbelt and his developer friends.
Crombie may suffer from the same GTA myopia. Her entire political career centred on Mississauga. Granted, winning legislative seats in the GTA is critical to any political party aspiring to govern Ontario, but there is much more to Ontario than the GTA.
Indeed, beyond the GTA, political tremors can occur. For example, how does NDP leader Marit Stiles feel after losing a legislative seat in Kitchener Centre to the Green party? Ford, despite his bluster about his concerns for all folks in Ontario, has demonstrated very little capacity to understand that Ontario is much more than just the GTA.
Crombie not only will be faced with Ford and the continuing dissolution process in Peel regional municipality but she also will need to make a major effort to shed the GTA introspection and find her way into the rest of Ontario.
Ignoring the boonies can be costly - just ask the NDP.
There is a very perilous political journey ahead for Crombie even if she can shake off her Mississauga mantle. Lloyd Brown-John is a University of Windsor professor emeritus of political science. He can be reached at lbj@uwindsor.ca.

Subject: Leadership; Local elections; Mayors; Legislatures

Brown-John: Perilous political journey ahead for new Ontario Liberal leader

Author: Schmidt, Doug

https://libproxy.wlu.ca/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/blogs-podcasts-websites/brown-john-perilous-political-journey-ahead-new/docview/2910863856/se-2?accountid=15090

Full text: In the torrential rush to prepare for Thanksgiving, Christmas, Black Friday, Cyber Monday, New Year's Eve, National Cookie Day, or Maple Syrup Day or Eat a Red Apple day you may have missed the news that Ontario's provincial Liberal Party has a new leader.
Bonnie Crombie won leadership of Ontario's currently rather obscure Liberal Party. She out-polled three other contenders. Her success on Dec. 2 incidentally coincided with "World Pear Day."
Crombie has been mayor of Mississauga, that rambling suburb of Toronto, since December 2014.

Crombie indicated in November on TVO that she would run for Ontario's legislature in 2026 - the next scheduled Ontario provincial election.
Crombie is an experienced political animal. Now a youthful 63 years of age, she earned a BA and an MBA from the University of Toronto. Her birth parents were of Polish origin.

In 2014, long-serving Mississauga mayor Hazel McCallion retired and she gave her support to Crombie, who won a close victory for the mayoralty job over a fellow city council member. She was re-elected with substantial majorities in 2018 and 2022.

With the City of Brampton and Town of Caledon, the city is part of the upper-tier Regional Municipality of Peel. Mississauga is where the big money resides, hence its desire to split from its municipal partners.

Crombie was not happy and said so in a press conference after Calandra and Ford's retreat from what she referred to as "the right thing," or what Hazel McCallion had wanted.

Crombie, now leader of a rump Liberal party in the legislature, continues to focus and fight a battle largely of interest to only those on the western fringe of the Greater Toronto Area (GTA).
Toronto-acculturated Tory Premier Doug Ford has yet to demonstrate that he has a working perspective of an entire province of Ontario as opposed to an introspective view of the world from the GTA. Consider the mess and scandal of the Greenbelt and his developer friends.
Crombie very well may suffer from the same GTA myopia. Her entire political career centred on Mississauga. Granted, winning legislative seats in the GTA is critical to any political party aspiring to govern Ontario, but there is much more to Ontario than the GTA.
Indeed, beyond the GTA political tremors can occur. For example, how does NDP leader Marit Stiles feel after losing a legislative seat in Kitchener Centre to the Green Party?
Doug Ford, despite his bluster about his concerns for all folks in Ontario, has demonstrated very little capacity to understand that Ontario is much more than just the GTA.
Crombie not only will be faced with Ford and the ongoing dissolution process in Peel regional municipality but she also will need to make a major effort to shed the GTA introspection and find her way into the rest of Ontario.
Ignoring the "boonies" can be costly - just ask the NDP.
There is a very perilous political journey ahead for Liberal leader Bonnie Crombie even if she can shake off her Mississauga mantle.
Lloyd Brown-John is a University of Windsor professor emeritus of political science. He can be reached at lbj@uwindsor.ca( mailto:lbj@uwindsor.ca ).
