Document 108 of 108

New year, same song from PM

Author: Kheiriddin, Tasha

Publication info: Kheiriddin, Tasha. 

https://libproxy.wlu.ca/login?url=https://www.proquest.com/newspapers/new-year-same-song-pm/docview/2910739749/se-2?accountid=15090

Abstract: None available.

Links: https://ocul-wlu.primo.exlibrisgroup.com/discovery/openurl?institution=01OCUL_WLU&amp;vid=01OCUL_WLU:WLU_DEF&amp;?url_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rft_val_fmt=info:ofi/fmt:kev:mtx:journal&amp;genre=unknown&amp;sid=ProQ:ProQ%3Acanadiannews&amp;atitle=New+year%2C+same+song+from+PM&amp;title=The+Windsor+Star&amp;issn=&amp;date=2024-01-05&amp;volume=&amp;issue=&amp;spage=A.5&amp;au=Kheiriddin%2C+Tasha&amp;isbn=&amp;jtitle=The+Windsor+Star&amp;btitle=&amp;rft_id=info:eric/&amp;rft_id=info:doi/

Full text: The year is only a few days old, yet it feels like we're stuck in 2023. Russia remains at war with Ukraine. Hamas is still at war with Israel. China is meddling in Taiwan's election. Artificial intelligence is eating our jobs. Social media is filled with rage about inflation, trans rights and Donald Trump. It's enough to make you hide under the covers, shut off your news feeds and watch cat videos until you fall into a fretful sleep.
Here in Canada, it also feels as though, despite the new year, we're grappling with the same problems, and no idea how to tackle them. In fact, our federal government's contradictory policies are making things worse.
Inflation continues to erode our standard of living, yet carbon tax increases will roll ahead on April 1. Housing remains in short supply, yet immigration levels are at historic highs. Geopolitical strife has increased, yet our military is short 16,000 personnel and if Canada were at war, we'd only have three days worth of ammunition to defend ourselves. Cue the cat videos, please.
In his year-end interviews, Prime Minister Justin Trudeau acknowledged Canada's military is woefully short on supplies, but blamed the situation on our contributions to Ukraine. On the carbon tax, he confirmed he will make no further exemptions beyond those announced for home heating oil.
As for immigration, while Trudeau acknowledged that, "We all bear some responsibility; this is a challenge that we have to work on all together," he added that it isn't the 500,000 new immigrants who are the problem: "It's the temporary immigrants that have spiked massively over the past couple of years that is putting so much pressure on the whole system."
Fair enough, but all these phenomena have happened under his watch. Eight years and three mandates have given the Liberals plenty of opportunity to assess the effects of their policies, and change course if things get worse instead of better. So given the mess we're in today, does Trudeau plan to change his tune? Apparently not. "This is exactly not the time to be slowing down," Trudeau told Global News. "The context we're in right now - where progress has become so fragile because of global and large macro events - is the time to be doubling down and rolling up our sleeves, and that's what I'm here for."
Actually, you're not here for that. As Albert Einstein purportedly quipped, "The definition of insanity is doing the same thing over and over and expecting different results." Canadians'mental health is bad enough; we don't need more nuttiness from Ottawa.
The government needs to start making the hard choices - the ones it doesn't want to make because they are politically unpalatable to its key voter bases, such as the progressive left and immigrant communities.
It should start by repealing the carbon tax to ease the pressure on Canadians' pocketbooks. It should not merely threaten to cap student visas to dubious diploma mills but actually do so, and stop allowing these students to be used as cheap labour by working more than 20 hours a week off campus. It should increase military spending on not just hardware but incentives for recruitment and support for military personnel, in order to make a career in the Armed Forces an attractive option for young people.
The irony is these policies may help the Liberals at the polls, not hurt them. Polls show a majority of voters in Liberal strongholds such as Atlantic Canada want more carbon tax carveouts. Sixty-two per cent of Canadians born outside of the country say immigration levels are too high. And two-thirds of Canadians - including 68 per cent in vote-rich Ontario - support increasing military spending to meet NATO's two per cent target.
This feels like a watershed year for many issues in Canada and around the world. Instead of doubling down, Trudeau should wise up. If these problems continue to fester into 2025, Canadians will roll up their sleeves and show him the door. Postmedia News Tasha Kheiriddin is Postmedia's national politics columnist.
